Objection of the Libertines Against Prophethood
Al-Nasafi presents the libertines' argument that prophethood entails foolishness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the libertines' rational argument against prophethood and why they consider it foolish.

Core Idea
The libertines argue that God's command and prohibition are foolish because they bring no benefit or harm to God.

Before we explore the refutation, we must first understand the objection. The libertines (al-khul'ā') present a logical critique: if God sent prophets, He would engage in commanding and forbidding, which is futile and below His dignity. This lesson lays out their argument and identifies the group.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the terms 'foolishness' (safah) and 'stinginess' (bukhl).
2
Identify the argument structure
The argument progresses: prophethood requires command/prohibition → that implies foolishness/stinginess → God is above that → therefore prophethood is false. Find each of these in the text.
3
Understand the libertines' identity
The passage describes the libertines as those who have cast off obedience and followed their desires, often hiding behind Sufism. Reflect on why this matters for the argument.
This is a group that uses rational argument to justify breaking religious laws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فذهبت طائفة إلى أنه لو ثبت لتضمن سفها، إذ فيه أمر ونهي وتكليف وتحريم وتحليل والله تعالى لا يليق به الأمر والنهي والتحريم، إذ الأمر بما لا نفع في تحصيله للأمر والنهي عما لا ضرر في فعله للناهي سفه، وتحريم ما لا حاجة فيه للمحرم بخل والله تعالى يجل عن السفه والبخل وإلى هذا يذهب طائفة الناس يسمون الخلعاء الواحد منهم الخليع، وهو الذي خلع عذاره عن ربقة الطاعة وأعرض عما تدعوه إليه عقله ويزجره عنه وانهمك في شرارته وطغيانه وطاوع هواه ونفسه الأمارة بالسوء في جميع ما يميلان إليه. وعند انكشاف القناع عن مكنون ضمائر كثير من المستتر بالتصوف، وارتفاع الخفاء عن مستور سرائرهم يعلم أنه من هذه الفرقة وهم القائلون بالإباحة طهر الله بلاد الإسلام عنهم وعصم الضعفة من عوام المسلمين عن أنواع مكائدهم وغوائلهم، وصانهم عن الوقوع في المبثوث من مصائدهم وحبائلهم.
A group held that if it [the sending of prophets] were established, it would entail foolishness, because it involves command and prohibition, obligation, making lawful and unlawful; and God, exalted is He, is not befitting of command, prohibition, and making unlawful. Indeed, commanding what no benefit is gained by the commander and prohibiting what no harm results to the prohiber is foolishness, and making unlawful what no need is in it for the one who makes unlawful is stinginess; and God, exalted is He, is exalted above foolishness and stinginess. To this view inclines a group of people called the libertines (al-khul'ā'), the singular of whom is al-khalī' — he who has cast off his rein from the rope of obedience, turned away from what his intellect calls him to and deters him from, immersed himself in his evil and transgression, and followed his desire and his soul commanding evil in everything they incline to. When the veil is lifted from the hidden secrets of many of those who conceal themselves under the garb of Sufism, and the concealment is removed from their concealed innermost thoughts, it becomes known that he is of this group. They are those who assert permissiveness (al-ibāḥa). May God purify the lands of Islam from them and protect the weak among common Muslims from their types of schemes and harmful machinations, and safeguard them from falling into the widespread snares and nets.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to prophethood, from a group called al-khul'ā' (libertines). Their reasoning: if God sent prophets, it would involve commands and prohibitions, which are foolish because God gains no benefit and suffers no harm. They see this as below God's dignity. The author then describes these people as those who have abandoned obedience and follow their desires, often hiding behind Sufism. The passage ends with a prayer against them. Note that this is just the objection; the refutation is not in this lesson.

Key Terms
الخلعاء
الإباحة
الأمر والنهي
سفه
بخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Libertines are the same as Brahmins.
Correction: They are different groups with different arguments.

Checkpoint
According to the libertines, why is sending prophets foolish?
A) Because prophets sometimes make mistakes
B) Because it involves God in command and prohibition, which they consider foolish
C) Because people already have all knowledge
D) Because prophets are unnecessary for salvation

Key Takeaway
The libertines argue that prophethood is irrational because it would involve God in futile commanding and prohibiting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The libertines rationalize their disobedience by claiming that God's commands are foolish. In what specific area of your life have you subtly accused God of being unreasonable or burdensome? Will you repent and submit fully today?
?
They hid behind the label of Sufism. What religious label or excuse do you hide behind to avoid full obedience? What is the real motive of your heart when you justify your sin?
?
The passage says they 'followed their desire and their soul commanding evil'. When did you last knowingly choose your desire over what you knew Allah commanded? What concrete step will you take tonight to break that pattern?

The Brahmins' Objection: Intellect is Sufficient
Al-Nasafi presents the Brahmins' argument that intellect suffices for obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Brahmins' argument that human intellect can independently know good and evil, challenging the necessity of prophethood.

Core Idea
If intellect can fully know good and evil, prophets become redundant.

Before refuting the Brahmins, Al-Nasafi lays out their claim in its strongest form. They agree that God commands and forbids, but insist that He has already equipped human intellects with complete knowledge of good and evil. This lesson examines that premise and its radical implication for the role of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the translation. Note that the Brahmins accept divine commands but argue intellect is sufficient.
The passage is a single doctrinal unit presenting an objection.
2
Identify the key claims
Break down the passage into three parts: (1) God has commands and prohibitions, (2) actions are good or evil, (3) intellects already know all good and evil and are naturally inclined or averse.
3
Understand the implication
Ask: If intellect already knows everything about good and evil, what purpose do prophets serve? The Brahmins conclude that people are obligated by their intellects alone.
This is the core of their objection against prophethood.
4
Prepare for refutation
Consider what might be weak in this argument. The passage does not give the refutation, but thinking critically now will help when you study the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت طائفة إلى أن الله تعالى على عباده أوامر ونواهي وأفعالهم منقسمة إلى المحاسن والقبائح، والمحاسن مأمور بها والقبائح مزجور عنها. غير أن الله تعالى أودع في عقول البشر العلم بجميع المحاسن وجبلها على الميل إليها، والمعرفة بجميع القبائح وطبعها على النفور عنها، فكانوا مبتلين مكلفين بعقولهم.
And a group held that God, exalted is He, has commands and prohibitions upon His servants, and their actions are divided into good and evil; good actions are commanded and evil actions are forbidden. However, God, exalted is He, has placed in the intellects of humans knowledge of all good actions and instilled in them an inclination towards them, and knowledge of all evil actions and ingrained in them aversion from them. So they are tested and obligated by their intellects.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, from the Brahmins (al-barāhima). They agree that there are commands and prohibitions, and that actions are good or evil. But they argue that God has already given humans intellects that know all good and evil and incline accordingly. Therefore, people are obligated and tested by their intellects alone, eliminating the need for prophets. This is a summary of their position; the author's refutation is not included in this lesson.

Key Terms
البراهمة
العقل
المحاسن
القبائح
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Brahmins' main argument against the necessity of prophets?
A) Prophets are corrupt
B) Human intellects are sufficient to know good and evil
C) God does not command anything
D) Miracles are impossible

Key Takeaway
The Brahmins' objection rests on the claim that human intellect can fully and correctly know good and evil, rendering prophets redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you decide what is right or wrong, do you often rely on your own reasoning without consulting revelation? How has this lesson exposed the hidden assumption that your intellect is sufficient? Will you change how you approach moral decisions today?
?
Think of a sin you committed while knowing it was wrong. Why did your intellect's knowledge not prevent you? Does this experience reveal a flaw in the Brahmins' claim that innate inclination toward good is enough? What does that mean for your need for revelation and prophetic guidance?
?
If you truly believed that your intellect could fully and correctly know good and evil without any external guidance, would you still feel the same urgency to follow the Quran and Sunnah? What would be missing in your relationship with God?

Refuting the Claim that Prophethood Entails Foolishness
Presents a group's argument and identifies them as the khula'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that prophethood entails foolishness (safah) and how the author identifies the proponents of this view as the khula'a (libertines).

Core Idea
Some deny prophethood by claiming it implies divine foolishness—a claim rooted in flawed reasoning.

This passage addresses a specific objection to prophethood raised by a group known as the khula'a. They argue that prophethood, with its commands and prohibitions, would be beneath Allah if it brought no benefit to Him. The author refutes this by explaining the fallacy of their reasoning and exposing the true nature of this sect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysis, then read the English translation. Let the full argument and description sink in before dissecting.
Notice the strong language used against the group.
2
Identify the central argument
Focus on the group's reasoning: why do they say prophethood entails foolishness? Identify the two sub-claims about command (safah) and prohibition (bukhl).
They assume God gains no benefit from commanding or prohibiting.
3
Understand the terms safah and bukhl
Safah (foolishness) here means commanding without benefit to the commander; bukhl (stinginess) means forbidding without need. The author asserts God is above both.
These terms reflect anthropomorphic projections.
4
Recognize the khula'a and their traits
The author identifies the group as the khula'a, describing them as those who have cast off obedience and followed passions. Their doctrine is absolute permissibility (ibaha).
The description reveals their moral and intellectual deviation.
5
Reflect on the author's conclusion
Note the prayer at the end asking Allah to purify lands from them and protect the weak from their schemes. This shows the author's stance and the threat they pose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من فذهبت طائفة إلى أنه لو ثبت لتضمن سفها، إذ فيه أمر ونهي وتكليف وتحريم وتحليل والله تعالى لا يليق به الأمر والنهي والتحريم، إذ الأمر بما لا نفع في تحصيله للأمر والنهي عما لا ضرر في فعله للناهي سفه، وتحريم ما لا حاجة فيه للمحرم بخل والله تعالى يجل عن السفه والبخل وإلى هذا يذهب طائفة الناس يسمون الخلعاء الواحد منهم الخليع، وهو الذي خلع عذاره عن ربقة الطاعة وأعرض عما تدعوه إليه عقله ويزجره عنه وانهمك في شرارته وطغيانه وطاوع هواه ونفسه الأمارة بالسوء في جميع ما يميلان إليه. وعند انكشاف القناع عن مكنون ضمائر كثير من المستتر بالتصوف، وارتفاع الخفاء عن مستور سرائرهم يعلم أنه من هذه الفرقة وهم القائلون بالإباحة طهر الله بلاد الإسلام عنهم وعصم الضعفة من عوام المسلمين عن أنواع مكائدهم وغوائلهم وصانهم عن الوقوع في المبثوث من مصائدهم وحبائلهم.
Some group went to the position that if it (prophethood) were established, it would entail foolishness, because it involves command, prohibition, obligation, forbidding and permitting, while Allah Almighty is not befitting of command, prohibition and forbidding, because commanding that which holds no benefit in its attainment for the commander, and prohibiting that which holds no harm in its doing for the prohibiter, is foolishness, and forbidding that which has no need for the forbidder is stinginess, and Allah Almighty is exalted above foolishness and stinginess. To this goes a group of people called the khula'a—the singular of whom is khali'—who is one who has cast off his halter from the rope of obedience, and turned away from what his reason calls him to and what deters him from, and immersed himself in his evil and tyranny, and obeyed his passion and his soul commanding evil in all that they incline to. And when the veil is lifted from the hidden consciences of many of those who conceal themselves by Sufism, and the concealment is removed from the hidden secrets, it is known that he is of this sect, and they are those who say absolute permissibility (ibaha). May Allah purify the lands of Islam from them and protect the weak from the common Muslims from their various schemes and calamities, and preserve them from falling into the spread out of their traps and snares.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of a group that denies prophethood, claiming it would entail foolishness (safah). Their reasoning: prophethood involves God commanding, prohibiting, obligating, and declaring things lawful/unlawful. But such actions are not befitting God because commanding what brings no benefit to the commander and prohibiting what brings no harm to the prohibiter is foolishness; likewise, forbidding what the forbidder does not need is stinginess (bukhl). God is exalted above these. The author then identifies this group as the khula'a (libertines), describing them as those who have cast off the rope of obedience, ignored the dictates of reason, and followed their evil passions. When their true nature is revealed, it becomes clear they are the proponents of absolute permissibility (ibaha). The author concludes with a prayer for protection from their schemes.

Key Terms
سفها
بخل
خلعاء
إباحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood entails foolishness because God's commands are unnecessary and bring no benefit to Him.
Correction: God's commands are for the welfare of creation, not for His own benefit; thus, no foolishness or stinginess is involved.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the khula'a argue that prophethood would entail foolishness?
A) Because prophethood involves commands that are not beneficial to God.
B) Because prophethood is a form of stinginess.
C) Because the khula'a are not rational.
D) Because prophethood is not needed.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is not a reflection of divine need but divine mercy; the argument of safah is based on anthropomorphic projections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that some religious obligations are pointless or burdensome? When that thought arises, how can remembering that God's commands are for your benefit—not His—change your heart?

The Brahmins' Claim: Reason Suffices for Guidance
Brahmins assert that reason knows all good and evil, making prophethood unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Brahmins' claim that human reason alone can know good and evil, making prophethood redundant.

Core Idea
The Brahmins argued that reason alone can guide humans, so prophets are unnecessary.

The Brahmins were a Hindu group who denied the need for prophets. They argued that God has placed knowledge of all good and evil in human reason, thus humans are obligated by their reason alone. This lesson presents their core claim and the initial argument that sending a prophet would be futile.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Brahmins' view
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how the Brahmins describe the role of reason.
The passage begins with the Brahmins' statement that reason suffices for prophethood.
2
Identify the Brahmins' core claim
Find where the Brahmins state that reason contains knowledge of all good and evil, and that humans are obligated by reason alone.
Key phrase: 'أودع في عقول البشر العلم بجميع المحاسن' (deposited in minds knowledge of all good actions).
3
Understand the argument against sending a prophet
The Brahmins argue that if reason already guides, sending a prophet would be an act with no benefit, which is foolish. Reflect on why they consider this a necessary consequence.
They conclude: 'فلو أرسل الله إليهم رسولاً ... لفعل ما لا جدوى ... وهو سفه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول البراهمة : العقل يغني عن الرسول]:
وذهبت طائفة إلى أن الله تعالى على عباده أوامر ونواهي وأفعالهم منقسمة إلى المحاسن والقبائح، والمحاسن مأمور بها والقبائح مزجور عنها. غير أن الله تعالى أودع في عقول البشر العلم بجميع المحاسن وجبلها على الميل إليها، والمعرفة بجميع القبائح وطبعها على النفور عنها، فكانوا مبتلين مكلفين بعقولهم، مأمورين منهيين بها وبالتكليف لهم كفاية، وبالوقوف بعقولهم على جميع ما يحتاجون إليه من المصالح، وقعت لهم الغنية عن الرسالة، فلو أرسل الله إليهم رسولاً وحالتهم هذه، أعني الاستغناء عن الرسل، لفعل ما لا جدوى ولا عاقبة تتعلق به حميده، وما هذا وصفه فهو سفه والله تعالى يجل عن ذلك. وربما يقررون هذا فيقولون بأن الرسالة إما وردت موافقة لما في العقل، أو مخالفة له. فإن كانت مخالفة فهي باطلة لأن العقل حجة الله وحججه لا تتناقض. وإن كانت موافقة للعقل فبالعقل عنها غنية فتكون خالية عن الجدوى فيكون عبثًا مع أن إرسال الرسل إلى من يعلم الرسول أنه لا يجيبه إلى ما يدعوه ويقابل رسوله بالتكذيب ويمد يده إليه بالضرب والتعذيب خارج عن الحكمة داخل في حد السفه. ولأنه لو أرسل لا فقد فضله على غيره من جنسه، وتفضيل الشيء على ما يجانسه ويساويه في الخلقة ليس بحكمة. وهذه الطائفة هم المعروفون بالبراهمة وهم قوم ينتسبون إلى الحكمة في الهند.
The saying of the Brahmins: Reason suffices for the messenger.

A group went to the position that Allah Almighty has commands and prohibitions upon His servants, and their actions are divided into good and evil, and good actions are commanded and evil actions are forbidden. However, Allah Almighty has deposited in the minds of humans knowledge of all good actions and created them with an inclination towards them, and knowledge of all evil actions and stamped them with aversion from them. So they were tested and obligated by their minds, commanded and forbidden by them, and the obligation is sufficient for them, and by stopping at their minds regarding all the benefits they need, they have become independent of the message. So if Allah were to send to them a messenger while they are in this state—I mean independence from messengers—He would do something that has no benefit and no praiseworthy outcome attached to it, and what is described as such is foolishness, and Allah Almighty is exalted above that. And perhaps they establish this by saying: The message either comes in agreement with reason or in opposition to it. If it is in opposition, it is false because reason is the proof of Allah and His proofs do not contradict. And if it is in agreement with reason, then reason makes it unnecessary, so it would be devoid of benefit and thus be futility, along with the fact that sending messengers to those whom the messenger knows will not respond to what he calls them to, and will meet his messenger with denial and stretch his hand to him with hitting and torture, is outside wisdom and falls within the boundary of foolishness. And because if He sends (a messenger?) then He has favored him over others of his kind, and favoring something over what is similar to it and equal in creation is not wise. And this group is the known Brahmins, and they are a people who trace themselves to wisdom in India.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the Brahmins' argument that human reason is sufficient, making prophethood unnecessary. The Brahmins are a group from India who claim that reason alone can guide humans. Their argument unfolds in several points: (1) God has placed in human reason knowledge of all good and evil, and created humans inclined to good and averse to evil, so they are obligated by their reason alone. Thus, they are independent of prophecy. (2) Sending a prophet when reason suffices would be an act with no beneficial result, which is foolishness, and God is above that. (3) They pose a dilemma: prophecy either agrees with reason or contradicts it. If it contradicts, it is false because reason is God's proof and proofs cannot contradict. If it agrees, reason makes it unnecessary, so it is futile. (4) Additionally, sending a prophet to people who will reject and harm him is contrary to wisdom and is foolish. (5) If God were to send a specific person as a prophet while others are equal to him, that would be arbitrary preference, which is not wise. The passage concludes by identifying this group as the Brahmins, who claim wisdom in India.

Key Terms
البراهمة (Brahmins)
العقل (reason)
المحاسن والقبائح (good and evil)
الغنية عن الرسالة (independence from prophethood)
السفه (foolishness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Brahmins are atheists who deny God.
Correction: The Brahmins accept God but deny the necessity of prophethood, claiming reason is sufficient.

Checkpoint
Why do the Brahmins think reason suffices for moral guidance?
A) Because reason is infallible.
B) Because God has placed knowledge of all good and evil in reason.
C) Because prophets are unreliable.
D) Because reason is divine.

Key Takeaway
The Brahmins' position is that reason alone can fulfill the purpose of prophethood, making prophets redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied solely on your own reasoning to decide right from wrong, ignoring what the Quran and Sunnah clearly command? Think of a specific situation. What was the outcome, and did it draw you closer to Allah or farther away? Are you willing to submit your reason to revelation today?
?
The Brahmins claim that reason knows all good and evil. Is there an area of your life—like ethics, worship, or daily transactions—where you act as if you don't need prophetic guidance? Identify one such area and commit to seeking the prophetic model before acting.
?
If you truly believed that sending a prophet would be 'foolishness,' how would your life change? Now, how does your actual belief in the necessity of prophets shape your daily choices? Are there gaps where you live as though prophethood is optional?

The Brahmins' Dilemma: Prophecy Either Agrees or Contradicts Reason
Brahmins present a dilemma and additional arguments against prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Brahmins' dilemma that prophecy either agrees with reason (making it redundant) or contradicts reason (making it false).

Core Idea
Prophecy is either unnecessary or false, both leading to its rejection.

The Brahmins formalize their argument into a dilemma: if prophecy agrees with reason, it is superfluous; if it contradicts, it is invalid. This lesson also covers their additional points about the futility of sending prophets to those who reject them and the unjustified preference of one person over another.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma passage
Read the passage beginning with 'وربما يقررون هذا فيقولون' carefully. Note the structure of the dilemma.
This is the second part of the Brahmins' argument.
2
Analyze the two horns of the dilemma
Identify the two possibilities: prophecy agrees with reason or contradicts it. Understand why each horn leads to the conclusion that prophecy is unnecessary.
If agreement: reason suffices -> redundancy. If contradiction: reason is God's proof, so contradiction is impossible.
3
Consider the additional arguments
Note the arguments about sending prophets to those who reject and harm them, and the charge of arbitrary preference. Reflect on how these strengthen the claim that prophethood is unwise.
These are presented as further evidence that sending prophets is foolish.
4
Learn about the Brahmins' identity
The passage ends by identifying this group as the Brahmins from India, who claim wisdom.
This helps contextualize the argument within Hindu philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول البراهمة : العقل يغني عن الرسول]:
وذهبت طائفة إلى أن الله تعالى على عباده أوامر ونواهي وأفعالهم منقسمة إلى المحاسن والقبائح، والمحاسن مأمور بها والقبائح مزجور عنها. غير أن الله تعالى أودع في عقول البشر العلم بجميع المحاسن وجبلها على الميل إليها، والمعرفة بجميع القبائح وطبعها على النفور عنها، فكانوا مبتلين مكلفين بعقولهم، مأمورين منهيين بها وبالتكليف لهم كفاية، وبالوقوف بعقولهم على جميع ما يحتاجون إليه من المصالح، وقعت لهم الغنية عن الرسالة، فلو أرسل الله إليهم رسولاً وحالتهم هذه، أعني الاستغناء عن الرسل، لفعل ما لا جدوى ولا عاقبة تتعلق به حميده، وما هذا وصفه فهو سفه والله تعالى يجل عن ذلك. وربما يقررون هذا فيقولون بأن الرسالة إما وردت موافقة لما في العقل، أو مخالفة له. فإن كانت مخالفة فهي باطلة لأن العقل حجة الله وحججه لا تتناقض. وإن كانت موافقة للعقل فبالعقل عنها غنية فتكون خالية عن الجدوى فيكون عبثًا مع أن إرسال الرسل إلى من يعلم الرسول أنه لا يجيبه إلى ما يدعوه ويقابل رسوله بالتكذيب ويمد يده إليه بالضرب والتعذيب خارج عن الحكمة داخل في حد السفه. ولأنه لو أرسل لا فقد فضله على غيره من جنسه، وتفضيل الشيء على ما يجانسه ويساويه في الخلقة ليس بحكمة. وهذه الطائفة هم المعروفون بالبراهمة وهم قوم ينتسبون إلى الحكمة في الهند.
The saying of the Brahmins: Reason suffices for the messenger.

A group went to the position that Allah Almighty has commands and prohibitions upon His servants, and their actions are divided into good and evil, and good actions are commanded and evil actions are forbidden. However, Allah Almighty has deposited in the minds of humans knowledge of all good actions and created them with an inclination towards them, and knowledge of all evil actions and stamped them with aversion from them. So they were tested and obligated by their minds, commanded and forbidden by them, and the obligation is sufficient for them, and by stopping at their minds regarding all the benefits they need, they have become independent of the message. So if Allah were to send to them a messenger while they are in this state—I mean independence from messengers—He would do something that has no benefit and no praiseworthy outcome attached to it, and what is described as such is foolishness, and Allah Almighty is exalted above that. And perhaps they establish this by saying: The message either comes in agreement with reason or in opposition to it. If it is in opposition, it is false because reason is the proof of Allah and His proofs do not contradict. And if it is in agreement with reason, then reason makes it unnecessary, so it would be devoid of benefit and thus be futility, along with the fact that sending messengers to those whom the messenger knows will not respond to what he calls them to, and will meet his messenger with denial and stretch his hand to him with hitting and torture, is outside wisdom and falls within the boundary of foolishness. And because if He sends (a messenger?) then He has favored him over others of his kind, and favoring something over what is similar to it and equal in creation is not wise. And this group is the known Brahmins, and they are a people who trace themselves to wisdom in India.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the Brahmins' argument that human reason is sufficient, making prophethood unnecessary. The Brahmins are a group from India who claim that reason alone can guide humans. Their argument unfolds in several points: (1) God has placed in human reason knowledge of all good and evil, and created humans inclined to good and averse to evil, so they are obligated by their reason alone. Thus, they are independent of prophecy. (2) Sending a prophet when reason suffices would be an act with no beneficial result, which is foolishness, and God is above that. (3) They pose a dilemma: prophecy either agrees with reason or contradicts it. If it contradicts, it is false because reason is God's proof and proofs cannot contradict. If it agrees, reason makes it unnecessary, so it is futile. (4) Additionally, sending a prophet to people who will reject and harm him is contrary to wisdom and is foolish. (5) If God were to send a specific person as a prophet while others are equal to him, that would be arbitrary preference, which is not wise. The passage concludes by identifying this group as the Brahmins, who claim wisdom in India.

Key Terms
العقل (reason)
الرسالة (prophethood)
العبث (futility)
السفه (foolishness)
الحكمة (wisdom)
التفضيل (preference)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Brahmins, if prophecy agrees with reason, then prophecy is:
A) True
B) Redundant
C) Contradictory
D) Necessary

Key Takeaway
The Brahmins use a dilemma to argue that prophethood is either contradictory or redundant, and add that sending prophets is futile and unfair.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a command from Allah because your reason told you it didn't make sense? What was that command, and how does this passage challenge your reliance on your own intellect?
?
When you share the truth with someone who you know will reject it, do you see it as futile? How does this passage's portrayal of the Brahmins' logic expose the arrogance of assuming you know better than Allah's wisdom?
?
If you believe Allah has favored you with guidance while others remain without it, do you feel a sense of superiority? How does the Brahmins' objection about unfair preference force you to examine your own gratitude and humility before Allah's choice?

The Barahima's Rationalist Challenge to Prophethood
Nasafi presents the Barahima's argument that prophethood is unnecessary and foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Barahima's rationalist objection to prophethood, including their premise of intellectual self-sufficiency and their dilemma that prophethood is either redundant or false.

Core Idea
Reason alone suffices — the Barahima's challenge to prophethood.

The Barahima, an Indian philosophical school, denied the need for prophets, arguing that human reason is fully capable of discerning moral and religious truth. Nasafi presents their challenge as a dilemma that seeks to prove prophethood either contradictory or redundant. This lesson examines their reasoning, which Islamic theologians had to refute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the overall argument before breaking it down.
Notice the use of key terms like العقل, السفه, العبث.
2
Identify the core premise
The Barahima begin by stating that humans are self-sufficient through intellect and obligation (taklif). Find this premise in the passage and understand why it leads to their rejection of prophethood.
This premise is the foundation of their entire objection.
3
Analyze the dilemma
The passage presents a dilemma: a prophetic message either agrees with reason or opposes it. Consider each branch and the conclusion drawn.
If it opposes reason, it is false; if it agrees, reason suffices so it is futile.
4
Examine the additional arguments
Beyond the dilemma, the Barahima argue that sending prophets to those who will reject and harm them is foolish, and that singling out one person as a prophet is unjustified favoritism. Identify these points in the text.
These reinforce the idea that prophethood is not only unnecessary but also senseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما مأمورين منهيين بها وبالتكليف لهم كفاية، وبالوقوف بعقولهم على جميع ما يحتاجون إليه من المصالح، وقعت لهم الغنية عن الرسالة، فلو أرسل الله إليهم رسولاً وحالتهم هذه، أعني الاستغناء عن الرسل، لفعل ما لا جدوى ولا عاقبة تتعلق به حميده، وما هذا وصفه فهو سفه والله تعالى يجل عن ذلك. وربما يقررون هذا فيقولون بأن الرسالة إما وردت موافقة لما في العقل، أو مخالفة له. فإن كانت مخالفة فهي باطلة لأن العقل حجة الله وحججه لا تتناقض. وإن كانت موافقة للعقل فبالعقل عنها غنية فتكون خالية عن الجدوى فيكون عبثًا مع أن إرسال الرسل إلى من يعلم الرسل أنه لا يجيبه إلى ما يدعوه ويقابل رسوله بالتكذيب ويمد يده إليه بالضرب والتعذيب خارج عن الحكمة داخل في حد السفه. ولأنه لو أرسل لا فقد فضله على غيره من جنسه، وتفضيل الشيء على ما يجانسه ويساويه في الخلقة ليس بحكمة. وهذه الطائفة هم المعروفون بالبراهمة
They are commanded and prohibited, and through obligation they are self-sufficient, and by their intellects they stand upon all the benefits they need, so they have no need of the message. If God were to send a messenger to them while they are in this state—I mean the state of being independent of messengers—He would do something that has no benefit or praiseworthy consequence attached to it, and whatever is described thus is foolishness, and God is exalted above that. Perhaps they articulate this by saying: The message either occurs in agreement with what is in the intellect, or in opposition to it. If it is in opposition, it is false because the intellect is God's proof and His proofs do not contradict. If it is in agreement with the intellect, then the intellect suffices for it, so it would be devoid of benefit, thus being frivolous. Moreover, sending messengers to one whom the messenger knows will not respond to what he calls him to, and who will meet his messenger with denial and extend his hand to strike and torture him, is outside wisdom and within the bounds of foolishness. And because if he sends [to one] he has preferred him over others of his kind, and preferring a thing over what is like it and equal to it in creation is not wisdom. This group are known as the Barahima.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the Barahima (Indian Brahmins) against the necessity of prophethood. The author first states their premise: humans are already fully capable of discerning right and wrong through reason and divine obligation (taklif) based on rational judgment, so they have no need for prophetic messages. They then offer a dilemma: If a prophet brings something contrary to reason, it is false because reason is God's proof and cannot contradict His revelations. If it agrees with reason, then reason suffices, making the message redundant and therefore a pointless action (عبث). Additionally, they argue that sending a prophet to people who will inevitably reject and harm him is foolish, and that singling out one person for prophethood when others are equal in creation is unjustifiable favoritism. Finally, the author identifies this group as the Barahima.

Key Terms
الرسالة
العقل
السفه
العبث
البراهمة
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Barahima denied all divine revelation and worship of God.
Correction: The Barahima accepted rational morality and obligations (taklif) but argued that reason alone is sufficient for guidance, making prophets redundant. They did not necessarily deny God's existence or moral law.

Checkpoint
According to the Barahima as presented by Nasafi, what is the consequence if a prophetic message agrees with the intellect?
A) It must be accepted as true.
B) It becomes unnecessary because reason suffices.
C) It confirms the intellect but still adds benefit.
D) It is a miracle.

Key Takeaway
The Barahima argued that prophethood is logically unnecessary because reason alone provides complete guidance, making any prophetic message either redundant or contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you acted as if your intellect alone suffices, ignoring the guidance brought by the prophets? What is one specific step you can take today to submit your reasoning to revelation?
?
When you hear an Islamic teaching that initially seems irrational, do you dismiss it like the Barahima, or do you trust that God's wisdom surpasses your understanding? How can you train yourself to respond differently?
?
The Barahima argued that sending a prophet to those who will reject him is foolish. Have you ever felt that sharing Islam with someone is futile? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you do this week to invite someone to the truth despite the possibility of rejection?

The Naturalists' Objection to Miracles as Proof of Prophethood
Nasafi presents the Naturalists' argument against miracle-based verification of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Naturalists' argument that miracles cannot serve as proof of prophethood, and why they consider it impossible to distinguish a true prophet from a false one.

Core Idea
Miracles cannot prove prophethood because either they are natural (copyable) or impossible (nature unchangeable).

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents the objection of the Naturalists (الطبيعيون) against using miracles to verify prophethood. They argue that even if prophethood is not inherently impossible, there is no reliable way to confirm it through extraordinary events. This challenges a fundamental claim of Muslim theology: that miracles are divine signs. Let's examine their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'العادة' and 'الطبيعة'.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The Naturalists argue that any miracle claimed as proof must either be within the realm of natural custom (العادة) or outside it. Find these two options and their consequences.
The first option leads to the possibility of imitation; the second leads to impossibility because nature cannot change.
3
Understand the sorcery counterargument
Some Naturalists add that even if God can violate nature, so can sorcerers, creating ambiguity. Note how this further undermines the reliability of miracles as proof.
This argument builds on the assumption that nature is fixed but God can intervene, yet such interventions are not exclusive to prophets.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that sending a messenger without verifiable proof is futile and foolish. Consider why the Naturalists see no benefit in prophethood under their assumptions.
This conclusion ties the whole argument together: if no proof is possible, prophethood serves no purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت طوائف من الناس إلى أن لا امتناع في ذاته ولا استحالة في نفسه لما فيه من تأييد العقول فيما تحتاج إلى معرفته وتسهيل لطريق وقوفها على ما بها إلى الوقوف عليه حاجة. غير أن الامتناع جاء من ناحية أخرى، واختلف هؤلاء فيما بينهم، فمنهم من زعم أن الامتناع ثبت لما أن لا فائدة فيه إلا بعد ثبوته، ولا طريق للوصول إليه والوقوف على ثبوته، لما أن دعوى المتنبئ تعارض دعوى النبي. ولا يمكن الفصل بينهما إلا بالدليل ولا دليل على ذلك، لأن ما اقترن به من الدليل إما أن كان خارجًا على العادة المستمرة وعلى ما عليه أمور الطبائع، وإما أن كان خارجًا عن ذلك؛ فإن كان خارجًا على العادة الجارية والطبيعة الثابتة فلا يعجز المتنبئ عن الإتيان بمثله. وإن كان خارجًا عن ذلك فهو محال إذ تغيير الطبائع وخروجها عما هي عليه أمر ممتنع والقول به محال وإلى هذا يذهب الطبيعيون وينكرون جميع ما ثبت بالتواتر من الأمور الخارجة عن المعتاد والأمر الطبيعي من نحو قلب العصا حية، وانفجار الماء من الصخر، وفلق البحر، وإبراء الأكمة والأبرص وإحياء الموتى، وكلام الناقة، والشاه المسمومة ونبع الماء من بين الأصابع وأشباه ذلك. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فلو أرسل الله تعالى رسولاً، وليس في مقدوره إقامة الدليل على صدقه فلا فائدة فيه بدون ثبوت رسالته، ولا وجه إلى إثباته إذ لا إلى إقامة الدليل عليه، لكان ذلك الإرسال خاليًا عن العاقبة الحميدة متعريًا عن النفع والجدوى، وهو سفه والله تعالى يجل عن الوصف به. وزعم بعض هؤلاء أن خروج الأمر عن المعتاد وقلب العادة والطبيعة في مقدور الله تعالى، إلا أن ذلك أيضا مقدور السحرة والمشعوذة فيقع بذلك تعارض بحيث لا ترجيح لأحد الأمرين على الآخر، وقد مر أن إرسال الرسول والحالة هذه خال عن العاقبة الحميدة متعريًا عن الجدوى.
Some groups of people held that there is no impossibility in its essence nor absurdity in itself, because of what it contains of supporting the intellects in what they need to know and facilitating the path to their standing upon what they have a need to stand upon. However, the impossibility came from another direction, and these groups differed among themselves. Some of them claimed that the impossibility is established because there is no benefit in it except after its establishment, and there is no way to reach it and to establish it, because the claim of a false prophet opposes the claim of a prophet. It is not possible to distinguish between them except by a proof, and there is no proof for that. For the proof that accompanies it is either outside the continuous custom and the way of natural things, or outside that. If it is outside the usual custom and the fixed nature, then the false prophet is not incapable of producing its like. And if it is outside that, then it is impossible, for changing natures and their departure from what they are is an impossible matter, and asserting it is impossible. This is the view of the Naturalists, and they deny all matters transmitted successively (mutawatir) that are outside the ordinary and the natural, such as the turning of the staff into a snake, the gushing of water from the rock, the splitting of the sea, the healing of the blind and leper, the reviving of the dead, the speech of the camel and the poisoned sheep, the flowing of water from between the fingers, and the like. Since this is the case, if God Almighty were to send a messenger, and he is not able to produce a proof of his truthfulness, then there is no benefit in it without establishing his message, and there is no way to prove it since there is no way to produce a proof upon it. That sending would be devoid of praiseworthy consequence, stripped of benefit and utility, and it is foolishness, and God is exalted above being described with it. Some of these claimed that the departure of a matter from the ordinary and the overturning of custom and nature is within God's power, but that is also within the power of sorcerers and magicians, so conflict arises with no preponderance of one over the other. And it has already been mentioned that sending a messenger in this state is devoid of praiseworthy consequence, stripped of benefit.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the Naturalists (الطبيعيون) against the possibility of verifying prophethood. They first concede that prophethood is not impossible in itself—it could theoretically support reason. However, they argue that there is no way to confirm a true prophet because: (1) The claimed miracle (proof) is either within the realm of ordinary natural powers—in which case a false pretender can replicate it—or it is a violation of nature, which is impossible because nature is fixed and cannot change. Thus, no reliable sign is possible. (2) Some Naturalists concede that God can perform miracles, but sorcerers can also do such things, creating ambiguity with no way to distinguish. Therefore, sending a prophet without a verifiable proof is futile and foolish. The passage ends by reaffirming the conclusion that prophethood would be devoid of benefit.

Key Terms
الطبيعيون
العادة
الطبيعة
المعجزة
المتنبئ
التواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Naturalists deny God's power to perform miracles.
Correction: The Naturalists do not deny God's power; they deny that miracles can be reliably distinguished from natural events or sorcery, making them useless as proofs for prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the Naturalists, why does a miracle that violates the natural order fail to prove prophethood?
A) Because God cannot perform such acts.
B) Because changing nature is impossible, so such a miracle cannot occur.
C) Because such miracles are only performed by sorcerers.
D) Because the prophet is not required to perform miracles.

Key Takeaway
The Naturalists argue that miracles cannot verify prophethood because any extraordinary event is either within natural capability (replicable by impostors) or impossible due to the fixity of nature, and even if possible, it would be indistinguishable from sorcery, making prophethood verification impossible and thus sending a messenger futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were present at the time of the Prophet ﷺ and saw his miracles, would your heart have submitted immediately, or would you have found reasons to doubt—perhaps similar to the Naturalists' philosophical objections? What does your answer reveal about your current level of certainty?
?
The Naturalists argued that prophethood is futile if not verifiable. Have you truly verified the message of the Prophet ﷺ in your own life, or do you rely on cultural habit? Make one specific change today to act on a proof you already know, such as a miracle or clear verse you often ignore.
?
You claim to believe in the Prophet’s ﷺ miracles, but do you treat the Qur’an—the greatest miracle—as a guide for your daily decisions, or do you relegate it to a relic? Name one area where you act like a Naturalist by demanding extra proof before obeying what is clearly commanded.

Refutation of the Brahmin Claim of Self-Sufficient Reason
Nasafi refutes the Brahmin claim of rational self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that human reason is insufficient for knowing moral obligations without divine revelation, specifically against the Brahmin doctrine of self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot fulfill the requirements of obligation; revelation is indispensable.

The Brahmins held that human reason can independently know good and evil, making divine revelation unnecessary. Nasafi counters that the previous argument about gratitude demonstrates reason's fundamental incapacity. This passage shows why the claim of rational self-sufficiency collapses under scrutiny.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Brahmin position
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the key claims: intellect knows good/evil, commands come from reason, reason is sufficient for obligation.
The term 'ghunya' means sufficiency or self-sufficiency.
2
Understand Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi says this claim is invalidated by the earlier demonstration that intellects are incapable of grasping the true nature of gratitude for God's blessings.
This connects to the previous lesson on gratitude.
3
Trace the logical flaw
Ask: Why does the incapacity of reason regarding gratitude prove that revelation is needed? Because without revelation, humans cannot know the proper measure of gratitude, which is an essential obligation.
4
Apply the principle
Consider how this refutation protects the necessity of revelation in Islamic theology. The Brahmins' error is assuming reason can cover all obligations.
This is a key distinction in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يبطل قول البراهمة القائلين بوقوع الغنية لوقوف العقل على المحاسن والقبائح، وحصول الأمر والنهي بها عن الأوامر الشرعية، وكون العقول كافية للتكليف إذ بينا أن لا كفاية البتة بذلك على ما قررنا من قصور العقول عن الوقوف على ما بينا.
And by this, the statement of the Brahmins is invalidated, who claim the occurrence of sufficiency (ghunya) due to the intellect's knowledge of good and evil (maḥāsin wa qabā'iḥ), and that command and prohibition are attained through it instead of revealed commands, and that intellects are sufficient for obligation, since we have shown that there is absolutely no sufficiency in that, based on what we have established regarding the incapacity of intellects to comprehend what we have explained.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Brahmins (Barāhima), a group that holds that human reason is self-sufficient for moral and religious obligation without need for divine revelation. Their claim is that reason can independently know good and evil (maḥāsin wa qabā'iḥ), and therefore commands and prohibitions can be derived from reason alone, making revealed laws unnecessary. Nasafi declares that this claim is invalidated (bāṭil) by the entire foregoing argument about gratitude. Specifically, the earlier demonstration that human intellects are incapable of grasping the true nature and measure of gratitude for God's blessings, and that an infinite regress necessitates revelation, proves that reason is not sufficient. The phrase 'we have shown that there is absolutely no sufficiency in that' directly refutes the Brahmin doctrine of ghunya (self-sufficiency). Thus, revelation (sam'ī bayān) is indispensable for knowing moral and religious duties.

Key Terms
براهمة
غنية
محاسن
قبائح
أمر ونهي
تكليف
كفاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human reason alone can determine all moral and religious obligations without any need for revelation.
Correction: Nasafi shows that reason is insufficient, especially regarding duties like gratitude, which require revealed guidance.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Nasafi uses to refute the Brahmins' claim?
A) The Brahmins are polytheists
B) Reason is completely useless in all moral knowledge
C) The earlier demonstration that reason cannot grasp the true nature of gratitude for blessings
D) The Brahmins deny the existence of God

Key Takeaway
The Brahmin claim of rational self-sufficiency is invalid because reason alone cannot fulfill the obligations known only through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you truly consult the Quran and Sunnah first, or do you rely on your own reasoning? What does your answer reveal about your trust in revelation?
?
If reason alone were sufficient, there would be no need for prophets. In what areas of your life have you been acting as if you know better than Allah's commands? What will you change starting today?
?
Think of a time you relied solely on your own intellect to justify a wrong action, ignoring what Allah has decreed. What was the consequence? How can you ensure that revelation becomes your primary guide going forward?

Taklif as the Purpose of Creation
Answers al-Khali' that without taklif, creation is foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taklif is necessary for creation to have purpose and wisdom, refuting the objection that divine commands are foolish.

Core Idea
Taklif gives meaning to creation; without it, existence would be vain.

Al-Khali' argued that divine commands without benefit to God are foolish. The author responds by showing that if taklif were removed, creation would be pointless, which is impermissible for Allah. This lesson examines that core argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the objection
Read the first part of the passage that presents al-Khali's objection and the author's initial response.
Notice the logical structure: objection followed by reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
The author imagines a world without taklif and shows that creation would be purposeless—folly. This is an impossibility for God.
This step establishes that taklif is not arbitrary but necessary.
3
See the conclusion
With taklif, creation becomes wise; anyone who thinks otherwise is ignorant. This refutes al-Khali's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد خرج الجواب عما تعلق به الخليع، أن الأمر والنهي بما لا نفع للأمر في تحصيله ولا ضرر على الناهي في فعل ما نهى عنه سفه، إذ قد بينا في أثناء كلامنا أن التكليف لو لم يكن وانعدم الثواب والعقاب ولم تصر الدنيا مطية إلى الآخرة التي فيها يجازى المحسن بإحسانه والمسيء بإساءته، صار تخليقه العالم ليوجد ثم يتلاشى ويثبت ثم ينعدم، ويفنى من غير أن يتعلق به غرض أو يحصل له عاقبة حميدة. وهذا النوع من الفعل سفه يذم فاعله، وتأبى الحكمة إطلاقه، وذا غير جائز على الله تعالى. فإذا صار تخليق العالم بواسطة التكليف حكمة، فمن ظن ذلك سفها فهو جاهل، فكذا في أفعال الخلق ما هو قبيح، فلو لم ينه عنه الصانع لكان ذلك إباحة له إذ الإباحة هي زوال الحظر والأمر عن محتمل التكليف، وإباحة القبيح سفه. ومما يؤيد هذا أن في القول بإزالة التكليف والثواب والعقاب إعطاء الأمان للنفس الأمارة بالسوء عن عواقب المطالبات، وإغراء للنفس السبعية التي من دأبها القهر والغلبة على العبث والفساد والنهب والإغارة، وسفك الدماء وتخريب البلاد وحصول التفاني وهذا كله سفه وورود المنع عن ذلك كله حكمة، فمن ظن أن المنع عن سفه والإطلاق له حكمة فقد قلب القصة. ذلك ويحققه أنه تعالى لو لم يأمر عباده بشكر ما أنعم عليهم ولم ينه عن كفرانه لكان فيه تضييع النعم وذا سفه، فلابد من الأمر بالشكر والنهي عن الكفران على ما سبق ذكره، وذلك هو التكليف. وقد سبق فى أول الفصل ما يوجب بطلان القول ببطلان التكليف فلا معنى للإعادة.
And the answer has emerged regarding what al-Khali' clung to, that commanding and prohibiting without any benefit to the commander in its attainment and without any harm to the prohibitor in doing what he prohibited is folly, for we have already explained during our discourse that if there were no taklif and reward and punishment were non-existent and the world did not become a mount to the Hereafter in which the good-doer is recompensed for his good and the evil-doer for his evil, then His creation of the world to exist then vanish and endure then become non-existent and perish, without any purpose attaching to it or any praiseworthy outcome resulting, would result. And this type of act is folly which its doer is blamed, and wisdom refuses to allow it, and that is not permissible for Allah Almighty. So when the creation of the world becomes wisdom by means of taklif, then whoever thinks that is folly is ignorant. So likewise, among the actions of creatures there is what is evil. If the Maker did not prohibit it, that would be permission of it, for permission is the removal of prohibition and command from one capable of taklif, and permission of evil is folly. And what supports this is that in the claim of removing taklif and reward and punishment is giving security to the soul commanding evil from the consequences of claims, and inciting the bestial soul whose habit is domination and supremacy to frivolity, corruption, plunder, raiding, bloodshed, ruining countries, and mutual annihilation. All of this is folly, and the occurrence of prohibition of all that is wisdom. So whoever thinks that prohibition of folly and its release is wisdom has inverted the story. And that is confirmed by the fact that if He Almighty did not command His servants to thank Him for what He bestowed upon them and did not prohibit ingratitude, there would be in that a squandering of blessings, and that is folly. So there must be command of gratitude and prohibition of ingratitude as mentioned before, and that is taklif. And earlier in the chapter what necessitates the invalidity of the claim of the invalidity of taklif has been mentioned, so there is no need for repetition.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the objection of al-Khali' (likely a Mu'tazilite figure) that divine command and prohibition without benefit or harm to God is foolish. The author argues that if taklif (religious obligation) and reward/punishment were absent, creation would be pointless and foolish. Therefore, taklif is necessary for wisdom. He extends this to human actions: if God did not prohibit evil, that would be permission of evil, which is foolish. Removing taklif would lead to chaos and anarchy. Finally, he adds that if God did not command gratitude and forbid ingratitude, that would be squandering blessings. Thus, taklif is essential for wisdom.

Key Terms
خليع
تكليف
ثواب
عقاب
سفه
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine commands are foolish because God gains nothing.
Correction: Divine commands give purpose to creation; without them, creation would be foolish.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would be the result if there were no taklif?
A) People would live in peace.
B) Creation would be purposeless and foolish.
C) God would be more merciful.
D) It would be better for humans.

Key Takeaway
Taklif is necessary for creation to have purpose and wisdom; without it, existence would be vain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live your life as if taklif is the very purpose of your existence? If not, what changes must you make today to align your actions with that purpose?
?
If you truly believed that every moment of your life is a mount to the Hereafter, how would you change the way you spend your time right now?
?
The passage says without taklif, creation would be folly. Do you ever feel that your life is pointless? How does this truth reshape that feeling into a sense of urgent responsibility?

The Necessity of Prohibiting Evil and Commanding Good
Argues that divine prohibition of evil is necessary to avoid folly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must command good and prohibit evil, and how removing taklif leads to chaos.

Core Idea
Without divine prohibition, evil would be permitted, leading to anarchy.

The author extends the argument from creation to human actions. If God did not prohibit evil, that would be permission of evil, which is folly. Moreover, removing taklif would incite the base souls to destruction. This lesson explores those points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the source passage carefully.
This section builds on the previous argument by applying it to human actions.
2
Understand the permission argument
If God does not prohibit evil, that is equivalent to permission of evil, which is foolish.
Permission (ibaha) is defined as removal of prohibition.
3
See the consequences of removing taklif
Without taklif, the evil soul and bestial soul are unleashed, leading to chaos and destruction.
The author lists examples: plunder, bloodshed, etc.
4
Conclude with the necessity of command
The example of gratitude shows that without command, blessings are squandered, so taklif is necessary.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever resented a command of Allah—like hijab, avoiding interest, or praying Fajr—feeling it restricts your freedom? According to this lesson, that feeling is a trick of the nafs ammara. Will you repent of that resentment and ask Allah to replace it with gratitude that He spared you from folly? Take 60 seconds to do that now.
?
This lesson says that if God did not command gratitude, it would be squandering blessings. Look at your life: are you thanking Allah for His blessings, or are you taking them for granted? Which blessing are you most ungrateful for? Decide one practical step this week to show gratitude for that blessing.

Maturidi Methodology and Refutation of Mu'tazila on Prophethood
Maturidi method of rational good/evil and refutation of Mu'tazili obligation to send prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on rational good and evil and how they distinguish themselves from the Mu'tazila regarding the obligation to send prophets.

Core Idea
Rational good and evil are discernible by reason, but not everything beneficial is obligatory for Allah.

This passage by Imam al-Nasafi presents the Maturidi methodology for discerning good and evil through reason. It then contrasts it with the Mu'tazili claim that sending prophets is obligatory because it is most beneficial for people. The author refutes this and warns his fellow scholars not to fall into that error.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where the passage presents the Maturidi view on rational good/evil and the Mu'tazili view on obligation of sending prophets.
Notice the contrast introduced by 'فأما المعتزلة فإنهم يقولون'.
3
Note the author's rejection
The author declares the Mu'tazili speech corrupt and promises to refute it later. Ask yourself why it is corrupt.
The key is the concept of al-aslah (the most beneficial).
4
Reflect on the warning
The author advises Maturidi scholars to be careful not to fall into the Mu'tazili error when proving prophethood. Why is this important?
This shows the fine line between rational good/evil and obligation upon Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على مثل هذه الطريقة يتكلم الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي، وكل من يقول من أصحابنا بإمكان استدراك الحسن والقبيح بالعقل. فأما المعتزلة فإنهم يقولون بوجوب إرسال الرسل، لما أن ذلك أصلح للعباد وما هو الأصلح للعباد فهو واجب التحصيل على الله تعالى وذلك كلام فاسد نبين فساده عند بلوغنا إلى إبطال القول بالأصلح، فينبغي لمن يتكلم من أصحابنا في هذه المسألة أن يكون بصيرًا بذلك ليصون نفسه في إثبات الرسالة عن الوقوع في القول بالأصلح، والله الموفق.
In this manner speaks Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, and everyone among our companions who says that it is possible to discern good and evil by reason. As for the Mu'tazila, they say that sending messengers is obligatory, because that is most beneficial for servants, and what is most beneficial for servants is obligatory for God to bring about. And that is corrupt speech; we will show its corruption when we reach the refutation of the statement about the most beneficial. So it behooves anyone among our companions who speaks on this issue to be insightful regarding that, in order to protect himself in proving prophethood from falling into the statement about the most beneficial. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi method and contrasts it with the Mu'tazili position. The author states that Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and his followers affirm the rational ability to discern good and evil. Then he notes that the Mu'tazila claim that sending prophets is obligatory because it is most beneficial for servants and thus obligatory upon God. The author declares this corrupt and promises to refute it later. He warns his fellow scholars to be cautious not to fall into the Mu'tazili doctrine of 'al-aslah' (the most beneficial) when proving prophethood.

Key Terms
إستدراك الحسن والقبيح بالعقل
الحسن
القبيح
وجوب إرسال الرسل
أصلح
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Maturidis and Mu'tazila agree on the obligation of sending prophets because both use rational good and evil.
Correction: The passage shows Maturidis affirm rational good/evil but reject the Mu'tazili conclusion that what is most beneficial for servants is obligatory for Allah to do.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why do the Mu'tazila consider sending messengers obligatory?
A) Because it is possible by reason.
B) Because it is most beneficial for servants.
C) Because it is commanded in revelation.
D) Because it is a rational necessity.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi method distinguishes between rational discernment of good/evil and the Mu'tazili claim that beneficial actions are obligatory for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something good in the world or experience a benefit, do you ever assume that Allah must continue to provide it? How does the author's warning against the doctrine of al-aslah challenge your expectations of Allah? What will you do to remind yourself that Allah is not obligated by your sense of benefit?
?
The author advises his companions to be 'insightful' to protect themselves from falling into error. In your own faith, where might you be drawing conclusions about Allah based on your own reasoning rather than revelation? Take a moment to identify one assumption you hold and ask: Is this truly from the Quran and Sunnah, or am I imposing my own logic?
?
If you genuinely believe that Allah is not obligated to send messengers or provide what you consider best, how does that affect your gratitude for His guidance? Spend a minute thanking Allah for the gift of prophethood, not because He owed it to you, but out of His pure mercy.

Why a Prophet Must Bring Evidence
Refutation of the Ibadiyya who accept a prophecy claim without evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why even a genuine prophet must provide evidence for his specific claim, and how this refutes the Ibadiyya position.

Core Idea
Prophethood may be necessary, but each claimant is contingent and needs proof.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi tackles a key theological problem: can someone be accepted as a prophet simply by claiming it? He argues that even if the institution of prophethood is necessary, each individual claimant belongs to the realm of possibility and therefore requires evidence. This directly refutes the Ibadiyya sect, which held that mere assertion demands immediate acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Note the logical flow from the necessity of prophethood to the requirement of evidence for each claimant.
Notice the contrast between what is necessary (Allah) and what is possible (the claim).
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between the necessity of prophethood in general and the contingency of a specific claim. This distinction is the core of the argument.
Without this distinction, the Ibadiyya error would persist.
3
Apply the refutation
Understand how this reasoning undermines the Ibadiyya view that a mere statement is obligatory to accept and that rejection is instant apostasy. The conclusion is that no contingent claim can be accepted without evidence.
This principle protects against accepting false prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو في نفسه واجب الوجود على ما قررنا ، فهو في حق كل فرد من أفراد الناس زمان جواز ورود الرسول وتعين هذا المدعي له في حيز الممكنات أيضا، إذ الدليل عند القائلين بوجوبه إن دل على ثبوته لا محالة، فلا دليل يدل على ثبوته في حق هذا الشخص المتعين، فلابد إذا لكل مدَّع يدعي ذلك في حق نفسه من إقامة دليل يدل على تعينه لذلك، لأن تعينه له من جملة الممكنات فلا يثبت إلا بدليل يوجب ثبوته. وبهذا يعرف بطلان قول الإباضية من الخوارج أن مجرد قول الرسول واجب القبول وأن تعرى عن الدليل، وأن الراد عليه يكفر في ساعته. إذ لا ثبوت لما جاز عليه العدم إلا بدليل يوجب ثبوته.
He is in Himself necessary of existence according to what we have established. Therefore, with respect to every individual of people at the time of the possibility of the coming of the messenger and the specification of this claimant for him, it is also in the realm of possibles. Since the evidence according to those who affirm its necessity indicates its existence necessarily, there is no evidence that indicates its existence with respect to this specific person. Therefore, every claimant who claims that with respect to himself must provide evidence that indicates his specification for that, because his specification for it is among the possibles, so it is not established except by evidence that necessitates its existence. And by this the falsehood of the saying of the Ibadiyya among the Khawarij is known: that the mere statement of the messenger is obligatory to accept even if devoid of evidence, and that the one who rejects him is an apostate immediately. For there is no existence for that which admits non-existence except by evidence that necessitates its existence.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage establishes a necessary principle: even if the concept of prophethood itself is necessary, each individual claimant resides in the realm of possibility and requires specific evidence. It refutes the Ibadiyya sect which holds that a mere claim commands immediate acceptance and that rejection is instant disbelief. The reasoning is that anything contingent (mumkin) cannot be established without a proof that necessitates it.

Key Terms
إباضية
واجب الوجود
دليل
ممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because prophethood is a necessary institution, any claim to it must be accepted without evidence.
Correction: Prophethood in general may be necessary, but each specific claimant is a contingent matter and requires proof.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to refute the Ibadiyya claim that a mere statement of a prophet is obligatory to accept?
A) The necessity of prophethood in itself
B) That the specific claim is contingent and requires evidence
C) That the prophet is infallible
D) That miracles are impossible

Key Takeaway
Even if prophethood is necessary, each claimant must bring evidence because his claim is among possibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a religious claim that appeals to you, do you examine the evidence or do you accept it because it feels right? Think of a specific belief you hold without clear proof. What would it cost you to seek the evidence tonight?
?
The Ibadiyya considered a rejecter of an evidenced prophet as an apostate. But do you treat people who reject your own unsupported opinions as if they are wrong? Whose testimony have you dismissed without giving them a hearing? Will you apologize or reconsider?
?
You have just learned that even a prophet must bring proof. How does this standard expose your own laziness in matters of faith? What is one teaching you have accepted blindly that you now commit to verifying from the Quran and authentic Sunnah this week?

Qur'anic Evidence for the Oneness of the Creator
The author references Qur'anic proofs of God's oneness and refutation of Jews, Christians, and polytheists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'an contains profound rational proofs for the oneness of God, as evidenced by the author's reference to specific Surahs and his admiration of the Qur'an's argumentative power.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is the ultimate source of rational theology.

In this passage, the author points to the Qur'anic evidence for tawhid (the oneness of the Maker) that has been presented earlier. He specifically mentions Surah al-Ma'idah and Surah al-An'am as containing refutations of Jews, Christians, and polytheists. He then remarks that even the most skilled theologian would be amazed at the level of argumentation in the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice the author's reference to previous evidence and his concluding remark.
The passage is short but dense in meaning.
2
Identify the Surahs and groups refuted
The author specifies Surah al-Ma'idah and Surah al-An'am as containing disputations with Jews, Christians, and polytheists. Recall the content of these Surahs.
You may want to open these Surahs and read relevant verses.
3
Reflect on the author's amazement
The author says that even an expert in kalam would marvel at the Qur'an's arguments. Think about why he says this and what it implies about the Qur'an's authority.
This shows the Qur'an is not just a book of laws but also a source of deep rational arguments.

Source Text

دلالة توحيد الصانع على ما سبق ذكر بعضها في إثبات وحدانية الصانع ومحاجة اليهود والنصاري والمشركين على ما تتضمن سورتا المائدة والأنعام، وذلك على وجه يتعجب المبرز في صنعة الكلام الماهر في وجوه الخصام من ذلك.
Evidence for the oneness of the Maker, as some of it has previously been mentioned in establishing the unicity of the Maker and the disputation with the Jews, Christians, and polytheists, as contained in the two Surahs al-Ma'idah and al-An'am, and that in a manner that the excellent one in the craft of kalam, skilled in the aspects of argument, would marvel at it.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage references another type of evidence from the Qur'an: evidence for the oneness (tawhid) of the Creator. The author says that some of these proofs were already mentioned earlier in the context of establishing God's unicity and refuting Jews, Christians, and polytheists, specifically in Surah al-Ma'idah and Surah al-An'am. He then comments that the Qur'anic arguments are so powerful that even a highly skilled theologian (mubriz fi san'at al-kalam) would marvel at them. This underscores the Qur'an's superiority in rational argumentation.

Key Terms
توحيد الصانع (oneness of the Maker)
محاجة (disputation)
صنعة الكلام (craft of kalam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Qur'an does not engage in detailed theological debates.
Correction: The author shows that the Qur'an, particularly in Surah al-Ma'idah and al-An'am, directly refutes the claims of Jews, Christians, and polytheists, demonstrating its deep engagement with theological arguments.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which Surahs contain the disputation with Jews, Christians, and polytheists?
A) Al-Baqarah and Al-Imran
B) Al-Ma'idah and Al-An'am
C) Al-A'raf and Al-Anfal
D) Al-Kahf and Maryam

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's arguments for monotheism are so profound that they astonish even the most skilled theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says the Qur'an's arguments amaze even expert theologians. When was the last time you felt truly amazed by a Quranic verse? What holds you back from consistently reading with that level of wonder? Commit to one small change in your daily Qur'an reading that invites awe.
?
This passage mentions disputation with Jews, Christians, and polytheists. Is your faith strong enough to engage honestly with opposing views, or do you avoid them out of fear? What step can you take this week to understand and respectfully challenge a belief that contradicts your own, as modeled by the Qur'an?
?
The 'craft of kalam' is a skill to be honed. Are you content with a superficial understanding of your faith, or have you decided to become a student who can articulate Islamic beliefs with clarity? What concrete action will you take to deepen your theological knowledge starting today?
