Bishr and Muhammad ibn Shabib on Stillness and Motion
Bishr and Ibn Shabib on permanence of stillness, motion, and color

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opposing views of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir and Muhammad ibn Shabib on whether stillness and motion can remain over time.

Core Idea
Bishr believed stillness and color remain until replaced by an opposite; Ibn Shabib denied any permanence of motion or stillness.

The Mu'tazilites disagreed sharply on whether certain accidents like stillness and motion could persist. Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir argued that stillness remains unless the body moves, and color remains unless it changes to its opposite. Muhammad ibn Shabib, however, categorically denied that motion and stillness can remain at all. This lesson explores their contrasting positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the views of Bishr and Ibn Shabib
Read the Arabic and translation. Note how Bishr specifies exceptions for the persistence of stillness and color.
Bishr uses the phrase 'إلا بخروج الجسم منه إلى حركة' (except by the body's exit from it into motion).
2
Compare the two scholars
Bishr allows permanence of stillness and color until an opposite occurs. Ibn Shabib rejects permanence of both motion and stillness outright. What is the key difference?
Ibn Shabib's position is more restrictive: no motion or stillness can remain at all.
3
Reflect on the implications
If Ibn Shabib is correct, then every moment of motion or stillness is a new creation. How does this fit with occasionalist views?

Under what condition does color perish according to Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Bishr believed that stillness and color persist until an opposite arrives. In your own life, what spiritual 'stillness' (like consistency in prayer) have you assumed will remain unless you actively introduce its opposite? What 'opposite' are you avoiding introducing?
?
Ibn Shabib viewed motion and stillness as momentary—gone as soon as they appear. Consider a moment of sincere dhikr or a good deed you performed today: do you live with the awareness that it was a fleeting gift, not guaranteed to return? How does that urgency change your next action?
?
Both scholars debated the persistence of accidents, but your own attributes of faith (iman) and character—are they lasting until replaced by sin, or do they require constant renewal? Will you renew your intention and reliance on Allah this very hour?

Al-Jubba'i and His Son on Accidental Persistence
Al-Jubba'i's classification of remaining and non-remaining accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i and his son's taxonomy of accidents: some are non-remaining, others can be preserved, and they disagreed about speech.

Core Idea
Al-Jubba'i distinguished between accidents that cannot remain and those that can be preserved, but he and his son split on the permanence of speech.

Al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim were influential Mu'tazilites who developed a detailed classification of accidents based on their capacity to persist. They agreed that many accidents like heat and life can be preserved, but disagreed on whether speech could remain. This passage outlines their nuanced positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Jubba'i and his son
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the list of accidents they consider non-remaining and those they allow preservation for.
The contrast between 'غير باقية' (non-remaining) and 'أجاز إبقاء' (permitted preservation) is key.
2
Identify the disagreement on speech
Al-Jubba'i allowed the permanence of speech (كلام), but his son prohibited it. Why might one allow it and the other not?
This points to a deeper debate about the nature of God's speech and created speech.
3
Consider the direct action distinction
Al-Jubba'i says the stillness one produces in oneself directly (مباشرًا) is non-remaining. What does this imply about actions and their duration?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشهور عنه أن سكون أهل الجنة وسكون أهل النار في آخر الأمر باق على الدوام، وكان يزعم أن ما يبقى من الأجسام والأعراض إنما يبقى من أجل بقاء لا في محل وذلك البقاء هو قول الله تعالى له: "ابق".
وقال بشر بن المعتمر : إن السكون كله باق لا يفنى إلا بخروج الجسم منه إلى حركة ، وكذا كل لون باق لا يفنى إلا بخروج الجسم منه إلى ضده، وأحال محمد ابن شبيب بقاء الحركة والسكون
وقال الجبائي وابنه إن الصوت والآلام والحركات والفكر والإرادات والكراهات والفناء أعراض غير باقية وأجاز إبقاء الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة والاعتماد والتأليف والألوان والحياة والقدرة والعجز والعلوم والاعتقادات وقال الجبائي : السكون الذي يفعله الحي في نفسه وكل ما يفعله في نفسه مباشرًا غير باق، وأجاز بقاء الكلام ومنع ابنه من بقاء الكلام، وقال ضرار بن عمرو والنجاري أن الأعراض التي هي أبعاض الجسم عندهما باقية وما سواها من الأعراض مستحيل البقاء
And the well-known from him (Abu Hudhayl) is that the stillness of the people of Paradise and the stillness of the people of Hell in the final state remains perpetually, and he claimed that what remains of bodies and accidents only remains because of a permanence that is not in a locus, and that permanence is the saying of Allah the Exalted to it: 'Remain.' And Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir said: Indeed all stillness remains and does not perish except by the body's exit from it into motion, and likewise every color remains and does not perish except by the body's exit from it into its opposite. And Muhammad ibn Shabib deemed impossible the permanence of motion and stillness. And al-Jubba'i and his son said that sound, pains, motions, thought, wills, aversions, and annihilation are non-remaining accidents, and they permitted the preservation of heat, cold, moisture, dryness, inclination, composition, colors, life, power, incapacity, sciences, and convictions. And al-Jubba'i said: The stillness that the living agent produces in himself and everything he produces in himself directly is non-remaining, and he permitted the permanence of speech, but his son prohibited the permanence of speech. And Dirar ibn Amr and al-Najjari said that the accidents which are parts of the body in their view are remaining, and what is other than those among the accidents is impossible of permanence.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration (عدّ) of the different positions held by major Mu'tazilite and other theologians on the question of whether accidents can remain (بقاء) over time. The text first reports Abu Hudhayl's view that the stillness of inhabitants of Paradise and Hell is permanent, and that bodies and accidents remain due to a separate attribute of permanence that is not located in a substrate. Then it mentions Bishr's view that stillness and color remain until the body leaves them for an opposite. Muhammad ibn Shabib held that motion and stillness cannot remain. Al-Jubba'i and his son made a distinction: certain accidents like sound and pains do not remain, while others like heat, cold, colors, life, etc., can be preserved. Al-Jubba'i also distinguished between actions produced by the agent directly (non-remaining) and speech (remaining), while his son denied that speech remains. Finally, Dirar and al-Najjari held that accidents that are parts of the body are remaining, but other accidents cannot remain. This enumeration sets the stage for the author's own argument.

Key Terms
إبقاء
عرض
كلام
فناء
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Jubba'i and his son agreed on all accidents.
Correction: They agreed on many classifications but disagreed specifically on the permanence of speech.

Checkpoint
Which accident did al-Jubba'i permit to remain but his son prohibit?
A) Heat
B) Color
C) Sound
D) Speech

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i and his son agreed on most accidental classifications but split on whether speech can remain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jubba'i distinguished between fleeting thoughts and lasting convictions. Think about your own mind: how many of your thoughts today were just passing fancies, and how many were rooted in a conviction that will endure? What one conviction are you willing to die for? If none, what does that say about the foundation of your faith? Starting now, what will you do to transform a casual belief into a firm conviction that shapes your actions?
?
The disagreement between father and son on speech reminds us that even the wisest can disagree. When you encounter a difference among scholars, do you feel threatened or curious? Have you ever dismissed a scholar's opinion simply because it contradicted your comfort? Today, examine one of your deeply held beliefs: is it based on the evidence or on the authority you prefer? If you were wrong, would you have the courage to change?
?
You have been given knowledge (sciences) and convictions (i'tiqad) which, according to al-Jubba'i, are accidents that can remain. But have you let your learning remain as a dead certificate on a shelf, or does it actively guide your decisions? Are your convictions merely intellectual, or do they stir your heart and move your hands? This week, choose one piece of knowledge from this lesson and act on it in a concrete way, so it becomes a lasting part of your character.

Dirar and al-Najjari: Accidents as Parts of the Body
Dirar and al-Najjari on accident permanence based on bodily part status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Dirar ibn Amr and al-Najjari's view that only accidents which are parts of the body remain, while other accidents cannot persist.

Core Idea
For Dirar and al-Najjari, a sharp divide exists: accidents that are constituents of the body remain; all other accidents perish instantly.

Among the diverse Mu'tazilite positions, Dirar ibn Amr and al-Najjari offered a unique criterion: accidents that are considered parts (ab'adh) of the body are capable of remaining, while all other accidents cannot persist at all. This lesson explores this boundary and its implications for understanding the nature of bodies and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read Dirar and al-Najjari's statement
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the phrase 'الأعراض التي هي أبعاض الجسم' (accidents which are parts of the body).
This is a distinctive concept: some accidents are literally parts of the body.
2
Identify the division
They divide accidents into two types: those that are parts of the body (remaining) and those that are not (impossible to remain).
Other Mu'tazilites did not make this distinction based on bodily composition.
3
Reflect on the consequences
If only accidents that are parts of the body can remain, then most accidents (like color, motion, etc.) are constantly renewed. How does this align with occasionalism?

?

?

The Karramiyya Dilemma on Baqa'
Poses a dilemma to the Karramiyya and pinpoints the real dispute with the Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed to the Karramiyya regarding permanence and the actual point of contention with the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The real debate is whether permanence is an additional attribute or identical to the essence.

Imam Nasafi here challenges the Karramiyya sect with a logical dilemma concerning divine permanence. After exposing the inconsistency of their position, he clarifies that the main dispute with the Mu'tazila is not about accidents being bodies but about whether permanence is an additional meaning beyond the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the argument's flow: a dilemma, then a shift to other groups, then the real point.
Notice the repeated use of 'if they say... then we say' structure.
2
Identify the dilemma for the Karramiyya
The passage presents two options: either the essence remains without permanence (absurd) or with permanence. Then it asks whether that permanence is additional to the essence or not. See how each option leads to a problem for the Karramiyya.
The Karramiyya claimed accidents can have permanence; this dilemma undermines that.
3
Distinguish the real dispute from other debates
The author mentions other debates (with al-Nazzam, Dirariyya, Najjariyya) as not central. The real dispute with the Mu'tazila is whether permanence is a meaning additional to the essence (our view) or not (their view).
This clarifies the main point of theological contention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للكرامية : إذا قال الله تعالى لذات "ابق". يبقى ذلك الذات ببقاء أم بلا بقاء؟ فإن قالوا : بلا بقاء، أحالوا ، وإن قالوا يبقى ببقاء، قلنا: فالبقاء معنى زائد على الذات، أم راجع إلى الذات؟ فإن قالوا: معنى زائد على الذات فقد انقادوا للحق وظهر بطلان مذهبهم في القول بجواز اتصاف الأعراض بالبقاء لاستحالة قيام الأعراض بالأعراض وإن قالوا: البقاء راجع إلى الذات والباقي باق لذاته لا لمعنى وهو مذهبهم، نبطل عليهم هذا الكلام بعد هذا - إن شاء الله تعالى.
والكلام مع النظام يقع في كون الألوان والطعوم والروائح والأصوات وغير ذلك أعراضاً أو أجسامًا، وذلك خارج عن غرضنا، وقد ذكرناه قبل هذا. وكذا الكلام مع الضرارية والنجارية يقع في بيان إحالة كون الأعراض أبعاضًا للأجسام، والكلام مع بقية المعتزلة يقع في أن البقاء هل هو معنى زائد على ذات الباقي أم لا؟ عندنا هو معنى زائد على ذات الباقي، وعندهم هو راجع إلى ذاته. هذا هو محل النزاع لتقدم الإجماع: أن الأعراض توصف بما كان
And it is said to the Karramiyya: If Allah the Exalted says to an essence 'Remain!', does that essence remain by a permanence or without permanence? If they say 'without permanence', they commit an absurdity. And if they say 'it remains by a permanence', we say: Is that permanence a meaning additional to the essence, or does it refer back to the essence? If they say 'a meaning additional to the essence', then they have submitted to the truth and their doctrine of permitting the qualification of accidents by permanence is invalidated because of the impossibility of accidents subsisting in accidents. And if they say 'the permanence refers back to the essence and the remaining thing remains by itself not by a meaning'—which is their doctrine—we shall refute this statement after this, God willing. And the discussion with al-Nazzam concerns whether colors, tastes, smells, sounds, and so forth are accidents or bodies, and that is beyond our purpose, and we have mentioned it before this. Likewise the discussion with the Dirariyya and Najjariyya concerns explaining the impossibility of accidents being parts of bodies. And the discussion with the rest of the Mu'tazila concerns whether permanence is a meaning additional to the essence of the remaining thing or not. According to us, it is a meaning additional to the essence of the remaining thing, and according to them, it refers back to the essence. This is the point of dispute, given the preceding consensus that accidents are described by what they were ...
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage first presents a dilemma to the Karramiyya regarding the permanence of essences. If God says 'Remain' to an essence, does it remain by a permanence or not? Both alternatives lead to problems that undermine their view that accidents can have permanence. The author then briefly notes that disputes with al-Nazzam (about whether qualities are accidents or bodies) and with the Dirariyya and Najjariyya (about accidents being parts of bodies) are not the main concern. The real point of dispute with the Mu'tazila is whether permanence (بقاء) is an additional meaning beyond the essence or identical to it. The author affirms that it is additional, contrary to the Mu'tazilite view.

ذات

Key Takeaway

?

Refutation of the Qadariyya's Attribute Distinction
Al-Nasafi exposes a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite distinction between essential and additional attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi exposes a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite distinction between essential attributes (sifat al-nafs) and additional attributes (sifat al-ma'na).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites' own criterion forces them to admit an additional attribute in creation.

The Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) distinguished between attributes that are inseparable from a thing's essence and those that are not. They used this distinction to deny that God has additional attributes. Al-Nasafi shows that their own criterion, when applied to the created substance, actually proves that at least one attribute—remaining (baqa')—is an attribute of meaning, contradicting their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the definition of the two attribute types at the beginning.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite definition
Find the sentence where the Qadariyya define 'attribute of the self' (sifat al-nafs) and 'attribute of meaning' (sifat al-ma'na). Understand the criterion.
The definition is crucial for the refutation.
3
See how they apply it to God
Note that they claim knowledge and power are attributes of the self for God because He cannot be without them.
4
Apply the same to the created substance
Observe the author's move: he applies the same criterion to a created substance (jawhar) and shows that knowledge and power are also necessary for it, yet the substance can be free of them (at times). This reveals a contradiction.
The text mentions that the substance can be free of the description of knowledge and power at first, but then necessarily described with them; this implies they are attributes of meaning, not self.
5
Focus on the attribute of remaining
The passage ends by applying the same logic to 'remaining' (baqa')—the substance can be free of it at the first moment, so it is an attribute of meaning, contradicting the Mu'tazilite position.
This is the decisive contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق هذا أن عبارة القدرية في إثبات التفرقة بين صفة النفس وبين صفة المعنى أن صفة النفس ما لزمه وصف المعنى ما جاز خروجه عنه، وعلى هذا بنوا قولهم بنفي الصفات عن القديم عز ذكره، وقالوا: لم يجز خروجه عن الوصف بالعلم والقدرة ولزم الوصف بهما، فكانا صفتي النفس دون المعنى، وقد رأينا الجوهر جاز خروجه عن الوصف بالعلم والقدرة ولزم الوصف بهما، فكانا صفتي النفس دون المعنى، وقد رأينا الجوهر جاز خروجه عن الوصف بالبقاء في أول أحوال وجوده بل وجب فتبين أنه صفة معنى على أصلهم أيضا، وهذه مناقضة لهم ظاهرة.
And that which verifies this is that the expression of the Qadariyya in establishing the distinction between the attribute of the self and the attribute of meaning is that the attribute of the self is that which is necessary for it, and the attribute of meaning is that which it is possible to be free of. And upon this they based their statement negating attributes from the Eternal, exalted be His remembrance, and they said: It is not possible for Him to be free of the description of knowledge and power, and the description of them is necessary, so they are attributes of the self, not of meaning. And we have seen that the substance is able to be free of the description of knowledge and power, and the description of them is necessary, so they are attributes of the self, not of meaning. And we have seen that the substance is able to be free of the description of remaining in the first state of its existence, rather it is necessary, so it becomes clear that it is an attribute of meaning according to their own principle as well. And this is an apparent contradiction for them.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) who distinguish between attributes of the self (sifat al-nafs) and attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'na). They define an attribute of the self as one that is necessarily inseparable from the thing, whereas an attribute of meaning is one that the thing can be free of. Based on this, they deny that God has additional attributes (sifat al-ma'na) because they believe His attributes like knowledge and power are necessarily concurrent with His essence and cannot be separated from it; thus they are attributes of the self. The author points out a contradiction: if we apply their criterion to the world, we see that a substance (jawhar) can be free of the description of knowledge and power, yet it is still necessarily described with them (in the sense that it is possible for it to acquire them? Actually the text says 'we have seen that the substance is able to be free of the description of knowledge and power, and the description of them is necessary'. This may refer to humans who may lack knowledge at one time and then acquire it; thus knowledge and power are not necessarily inseparable from the human essence. On their principle, knowledge and power would be attributes of meaning for humans, not attributes of the self. But they claim that for God they are attributes of the self. The same logic applies to remaining: a substance is able to be free of the description of remaining at the first moment, so by their own principle, remaining must be an attribute of meaning even for God. Hence they contradict themselves.

Key Terms
صفة النفس
صفة المعنى
القدرية
الجوهر
العلم
القدرة
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Mu'tazilites consistently deny all additional attributes (sifat al-ma'na) from God.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that their own criterion forces them to admit that at least one attribute—remaining (baqa')—is an attribute of meaning, so they cannot consistently deny all additional attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the Qadariyya, what distinguishes an attribute of the self (sifat al-nafs) from an attribute of meaning (sifat al-ma'na)?
A) An attribute of self is what the thing cannot be free of, while an attribute of meaning is what it can be free of.
B) An attribute of self is known through reason, while an attribute of meaning is known through revelation.
C) An attribute of self is shared by all beings, while an attribute of meaning is specific to God.
D) An attribute of self is eternal, while an attribute of meaning is temporal.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazilite distinction between essential and additional attributes collapses when applied consistently, undermining their denial of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they were defending God's transcendence by denying attributes, but their logic led to a contradiction. In what area of your faith are you relying on your own reasoning over what Allah has clearly revealed, and what step will you take today to submit your intellect to the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
Al-Nasafi exposes that even the Mu'tazilites' own criterion forces them to admit an attribute they denied. Are there any beliefs or practices you hold that, if consistently applied, would force you to accept something you currently reject? How will you examine your own consistency before Allah?
?
This passage shows the danger of building theology on human logic alone. When have you felt confident in your understanding of a religious matter, only to realize you contradicted a clear text? How can you cultivate humility and reliance on divine revelation in your daily life?

Refuting the Claim That Attributes Are Attributes of the Soul
Refutes the opponents' argument that attributes are attributes of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' claim that God's attributes are attributes of the soul, and how the author refutes it by exposing a contradiction using the example of substance and persistence.

Core Idea
Contradiction: opponents accept attributes of meaning for substances but deny them for God.

In this passage, the author tackles an argument from opponents (likely Mu'tazila) who deny that God has real attributes of meaning, claiming instead that His necessary descriptions are mere attributes of the soul. By drawing on their own principles regarding substances, the author reveals an inconsistency that undermines their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the key terms: صفات النفس and صفات المعنى.
2
Understand the opponents' position
The opponents argue that because God cannot be described without knowledge and power, these descriptions are necessary – hence they are attributes of the soul, not of meaning.
Attributes of the soul are not real distinct meanings but mere descriptions.
3
Follow the author's counterexample
The author brings up a substance (al-jawhar) and the attribute of persistence (al-baqāʾ). A substance can lack persistence initially, so persistence must be an attribute of meaning according to the opponents' own principles.
If necessity of description made it a soul-attribute, then persistence would also be a soul-attribute for substances, but opponents treat it as a meaning-attribute.
4
Identify the contradiction
Since opponents accept that persistence in substances is an attribute of meaning, they cannot consistently claim that necessary attributes of God are mere soul-attributes. This exposes a contradiction.
The contradiction is apparent (مناقضة ظاهرة).
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This argument defends the reality of God's attributes (ṣifāt al-maʿnā) and shows that opponents' reasoning is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنه، وعلى هذا بنوا قولهم بنفي الصفات عن القديم عز ذكره، وقالوا: لم يجز خروجه عن الوصف بالعلم والقدرة ولزم الوصف بهما، فكانا صفتي النفس دون المعنى، وقد رأينا الجوهر جاز خروجه عن الوصف بالبقاء في أول أحوال وجوده بل وجب فتبين أنه صفة معنى على أصلهم أيضا، وهذه مناقضة لهم ظاهرة.
From him, and upon this they built their statement negating the attributes of the Eternal, Exalted is His mention, and they said: It is not permissible for Him to depart from description with knowledge and power, and description with them is necessary, so they are two attributes of the soul (ṣifatā al-nafs) not of meaning (al-maʿnā). And we have seen that the substance (al-jawhar) can depart from description with persistence (al-baqāʾ) in the first states of its existence, rather it is necessary, so it becomes clear that it is an attribute of meaning according to their own principle as well. And this is an apparent contradiction (munāqaḍa) for them.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage refutes an argument by opponents (likely Muʿtazila) who deny that God has attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿnā) and instead claim that His attributes are attributes of the soul (ṣifāt al-nafs), meaning they are not real distinct meanings. The opponent argues: God cannot be described without knowledge and power, so those descriptions are necessary—thus they are attributes of the soul. The author counters by using the example of a substance (jawhar): a substance can be devoid of the attribute of persistence (baqāʾ) at the beginning of its existence; in fact it must be so. Yet according to the opponents' own principles, persistence is an attribute of meaning. Therefore, if they apply the same logic to God, they fall into contradiction because they accept that necessity of description does not imply it is an attribute of the soul; it could be an attribute of meaning. The author concludes that this is an apparent contradiction for them.

Key Terms
صفة النفس
صفة المعنى
جوهر
بقاء
مناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that if a description is necessary for something, it must be an attribute of the soul (not a real attribute).
Correction: The example of persistence in substances shows that necessity does not automatically make it a soul-attribute; it can be an attribute of meaning.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the opponents' argument contradictory?
A) Because they claim God has no attributes at all.
B) Because they admit that substances can have attributes of meaning, but deny them for God.
C) Because they say knowledge and power are necessary for God, but that does not prove they are attributes of the soul.
D) Because they reject the existence of attributes of meaning altogether.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' argument leads to a contradiction because they accept attributes of meaning in created substances while denying them for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological argument that seems consistent on the surface, have you ever stopped to test it with counterexamples the way the author tests the opponents' claim? Where in your own beliefs might you be applying double standards without realizing it? Will you commit, today, to actively search for hidden contradictions in your understanding of Allah's attributes and correct them humbly?
?
The opponents wanted to protect Allah’s transcendence, yet fell into contradiction. In your desire to avoid certain wrong beliefs about Allah (like anthropomorphism or negation), have you ever adopted a position that contradicts other clear truths? What would it cost you to abandon that inconsistency? Are you willing to pay that price?
?
This lesson exposes that even learned theologians can hold contradictory views while thinking they are consistent. How does that challenge your trust in your own opinions? What practical step will you take this week to examine one of your core beliefs about Allah and test its consistency with the Quran and Sunnah?

The Impossible Consequences of Denying Accidents' Non-Eternity
Nasafi refutes the claim that accidents are eternal and shows its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying that accidents are non-eternal leads to contradictions, including the impossibility of annihilation and the implication of the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
If accidents are eternal, neither bodies nor anything else can ever cease to exist.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla tackles a dangerous consequence of denying that accidents are temporally originated. If one insists that accidents are eternal, they must either become materialists who deny annihilation altogether or accept that their own arguments lead to the impossibility of any non-existence. Nasafi systematically shows the dead ends this position creates, even addressing a specific objection from the Karramiyya sect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target position
Read the opening line to see which view Nasafi is refuting. Notice that he draws an immediate parallel to the Dahriyya (materialists).
2
Follow the argument about bodies
See how the denial of accidents' non-eternity affects the possibility of body annihilation. Nasafi explains the Mu'tazilite theory of annihilation via an accident and why it fails.
Pay attention to the role of 'fanāʾ' as an accident not in a locus.
3
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that if accidents are eternal, all existents are necessarily eternal, which contradicts the consensus that only God is eternal.
4
Examine the Karramiyya objection
Read the objection that God's command 'be annihilated' can destroy an eternal accident. Nasafi dismisses it, though the text cuts off; infer why it's false.
Consider: if an accident is eternal in itself, can any command change its nature?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أن يجحدوا عدم الأعراض وادعوا فيها الظهور والكمون فيلتحقون حينئذ بإخوانهم الدهرية، وبطلان ذلك قد مر، مع أنهم لا يتجاسرون على إظهار ذلك خوفا من معرة السيف، وكذا على مذهبهم لا يمكن القول بانعدام الأجسام، إذ من مذهب من يناظرهم من الجبائية والبهشمية إذ انعدام الأجسام لا تكون إلا بحدوث الفناء وهو عرض حادث لا في محل مضاد للأجسام والجواهر، فيوجب عدمها في الثاني، وحدوث العرض لا في محل مستحيل، ولا طريق للإعدام عندهم إلا هذا فإذا تعذر القول بجواز انعدام الأجسام والجواهر والأعراض جميعًا، ولهذا أحال الجاحظ منهم عدم الأجسام، وأجمع العقلاء سوى الجاحظ أن كل موجود يمتنع عليه العدم فهو قديم، فصار قولهم مؤديا إلى القول بقدم العالم، واعتراض الكرامية على هذا الدليل أن العرض وإن كان باقيًا لذاته ينعدم لقول الله تعالى له افن باطل أيضا، لأن من مذهبهم أن الله تعالى متى قال للباقي: "فن". ينعدم في الثاني، وهذا فاسد، لأن وجود الباقي مع
Unless they deny the non-existence of accidents and claim for them [i.e., accidents] manifestation and concealment, thereby joining their brethren the Dahriyya (materialists). The falsehood of that has already passed, even though they do not dare to show that for fear of the disgrace of the sword. Likewise, according to their doctrine, it is not possible to speak of the non-existence of bodies, since the doctrine of those who debate them from the Jabāʾiyya and Bahshamiyya is that the non-existence of bodies only occurs by the occurrence of annihilation (fanāʾ), which is an accident that comes to be (ḥādith) not in a locus, opposite to bodies and substances, so it necessitates their non-existence in the second [moment]. But the occurrence of an accident not in a locus is impossible. And there is no way for annihilation according to them except this. So if it is impossible to speak of the permissibility of non-existence of bodies, substances, and accidents all together, for this reason al-Jāḥiẓ among them deemed impossible the non-existence of bodies. And all rational people except al-Jāḥiẓ agree that every existent that is impossible for it to cease is eternal (qadīm). So their statement leads to the statement of the eternity of the world. And the objection of the Karramiyya against this proof — that an accident, even if it is persistent in itself, ceases by the saying of God Most High to it 'be annihilated' — is also false, because it is part of their doctrine that God Most High, when He says to the persistent 'be annihilated', it ceases in the second [moment]. And this is corrupt, because the existence of the persistent with...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of those who claim accidents are eternal. It first addresses those who deny the non-existence of accidents and instead claim that accidents undergo 'manifestation and concealment' (a kind of occasionalism). The author dismisses this as joining the Dahriyya (materialists) whose falsehood has already been shown. He then argues that if accidents are eternal, bodies cannot be annihilated either, because according to the Mu'tazila (Jabāʾiyya and Bahshamiyya) bodies are annihilated by a discrete accident of 'annihilation' (fanāʾ) that occurs not in a locus. But an accident not in a locus is impossible, so annihilation is impossible. Therefore, if accidents are eternal, bodies and substances cannot cease, implying that all existents are eternal — which contradicts the consensus that only the eternal (God) is uncaused. The author then notes that even al-Jāḥiẓ, a prominent Mu'tazilite, deemed the non-existence of bodies impossible. The passage then addresses a specific objection from the Karramiyya: they claim that even if an accident is eternal in itself, God can annihilate it by His command 'Be annihilated!' The author rejects this as false because if the accident were truly eternal in itself, the command would not be effective; the argument then gets cut off, but the implication is that this would entail contradiction. The overall point is that denying the non-eternity of accidents leads to absurdities like the eternity of the world.

قدم العالم
كرامية
جبائية
بهشمية
جاحظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's command can annihilate anything, even if it is eternal in essence.
Correction: If something is eternal in itself, it cannot cease to exist, because non-existence would contradict its essential persistence. A temporal command cannot change an eternal nature.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the Karramiyya claim that God's command 'Be annihilated' can destroy an eternal accident?
A) Because the command would be ineffective if the accident is eternal in itself.
B) Because God does not command annihilation.
C) Because the accident would not hear the command.
D) Because the command would require a locus.

Key Takeaway
Denying that accidents are non-eternal forces one into absurdities: materialism, impossibility of annihilation, and ultimately the eternity of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation (an 'accident' of this life), do you try to pretend it is permanent by ignoring its transient nature? How would accepting its temporality change your reliance on Allah?
?
The Karramiyya thought God's power could overrule logic, but Nasafi showed that an eternal accident cannot be annihilated without contradiction. Are there areas where you try to 'fit' God into your own understanding rather than submitting to His absolute transcendence?
?
Nasafi warns that denying the non-eternity of accidents leads to materialism and the eternity of the world. In what ways do you cling to worldly things as if they were eternal, and what would it mean for you to truly believe that everything except Allah is fleeting?

Refuting the Karramiyya on God's Command Annihilating Accidents
Refutes Karramiyya objection using coexistence argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi refutation of the Karramiyya objection demonstrates the logical consistency of a persistent accident coexisting with God's command.

Core Idea
If command and existence can coexist once, they can coexist always.

The Karramiyya sect argued that God's command 'Be annihilated' would necessarily cause an accident to cease in the next moment. This passage refutes that by showing that if the command and the accident's existence can coexist in one moment without contradiction, then they can do so in any number of moments. The objection arbitrarily assumes the command must take effect in a later moment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully and then its literal English translation. Let the author's own wording and logical flow come through before analyzing.
2
Identify the Karramiyya Objection
The first part of the passage presents the objection: even if an accident persists by itself, God could command it 'Be annihilated' and it would cease in the next moment. Find and understand this claim.
3
Analyze the Refutation’s Logical Move
The author refutes by noting that if the command and the accident's existence can coexist in a single moment without opposition, then they can coexist in many moments. There is no reason to prefer the second moment for annihilation over the first.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the objection's logic would arbitrarily privilege one moment over another.
4
Reflect on the Conclusion
Consider how this refutation upholds the possibility of an accident persisting by itself despite God's command. The objection fails because it cannot explain why annihilation would occur at a specific later moment rather than all moments simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض الكرامية على هذا الدليل أن العرض وإن كان باقيًا لذاته ينعدم لقول الله تعالى له افن باطل أيضا، لأن من مذهبهم أن الله تعالى متى قال للباقي: افن. ينعدم في الثاني، وهذا فاسد، لأن وجود الباقي مع قول الله تعالى له: افن لما جاز زمانا واحدًا ولم يتنافيا جاز في أزمنة كثيرة، وليس الفناء في الثاني والثالث بأولى منه في الأول.
And the objection of the Karramiyya to this argument — that the accident, even if it were persistent by itself, would become non-existent due to God's saying to it 'Be annihilated' — is also false, because it is according to their doctrine that whenever God says to the persistent thing 'Be annihilated', it becomes non-existent in the second. And this is corrupt, because the existence of the persistent thing along with God's saying to it 'Be annihilated' — since it is permissible for one time and they do not oppose each other — is permissible in many times. And annihilation in the second and third is not more likely than it in the first.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Karramiyya object to the previous proof: they claim that even if an accident persists by itself, God could command it to annihilate (say 'kun fan' — be annihilated), causing it to cease. The author refutes this: if God's command and the existence of the accident can coexist in a single moment without opposition, then they can coexist in many moments. There is no reason why annihilation should occur in the second moment rather than the first or third. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
كرامية
إفناء
أمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's command to annihilate must take effect in the next instant, overriding persistence.
Correction: The command can coexist with the accident's existence in the same moment, so there is no necessity for it to cause annihilation later.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the Karramiyya objection that God's command can annihilate a persistent accident?
A) God never commands annihilation.
B) The command and existence cannot coexist.
C) If they coexist in one moment, they can in many.
D) The accident is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya objection fails because coexistence of command and existence in one moment implies coexistence in all moments, so persistence is not disproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though a single moment of failure annihilates all your past persistence in good deeds? The author shows that coexistence in one moment implies many moments—how does this challenge your view of God's mercy and your own spiritual continuity?
?
The Karramiyya assumed that God's command must take effect in the next moment. In your life, do you assume that God's commands will only manifest later, ignoring that they can coexist with your current state? How would your daily actions change if you believed that God's command to be righteous can be fulfilled in this very moment?

Why Accidents Cannot Persist: A Maturidi Refutation
Questions Mu'tazilites on persistence of accidents and refutes Bahshamiyya on speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi objection to Mu'tazilite views on the persistence of accidents and speech, and the refutation of the Bahshamiyya argument about speech.

Core Idea
The impossibility of accidents persisting is rooted in their nature, not in contingent needs.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi challenges the Mu'tazilites on two fronts: first, questioning why they deny persistence to certain accidents if persistence is by essence, and second, refuting the Bahshamiyya claim that speech cannot persist due to the need for interruption. The argument hinges on a fundamental principle about the nature of attributes and their relation to persistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the direct questioning style directed at specific Mu'tazilite figures.
2
Identify the two main objections
The passage first questions al-Jabba'i and his son about general accidents, then questions Abu Hashim about speech. Identify the principle used: an attribute that cannot exist at inception cannot persist later.
The principle is key: 'ما لا يصح حدوث شيء فيه أوله أو به لم يصح بغير الوصف عليه بعد الوجود'
3
Understand the refutation of Bahshamiyya
The Bahshamiyya argued that speech does not persist because it needs to be interrupted and followed by similar speech. The author counters that if that were the reason, a single letter could persist, which they deny. The real reason is the impossibility of persistence for such accidents.
This shows the fallacy in their reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للجبائي وابنه إذا كان الباقي باقيًا لذاته، فما الذي منع من القول بجواز بقاء الصوات والآلام والحركات والفكر والإرادات والكراهات والفناء؟ وكذا يقال لأبي هاشم: ما الذي منع من القول بجواز بقاء الكلام؟ ويحرر هذا، ويقول: ما لا يصح حدوث شيء فيه أوله أو به لم يصح بغير الوصف عليه بعد الوجود، كهذه الأعراض التي وافقتمونا على استحالة القول ببقائها، ولو قلنا ببقاء الأعراض لتغير الوصف عليها لما بينا أنها في حالة الحدوث ليس بموصوفة بالبقاء. ولا معنى لقول البهشمية إن الكلام إنما لا ينفي للحاجة إلى قطعه واتباعه بجنسه على صورة النظم والتأليف، لأن ما يتبعه إذا لم يكن ضدا له حاصلاً في محله لا يوجب المنع من بقائه؛ ولأن العلة المانعة عن بقائه لو كان هذا لكان يجوز بقاء الحرف الواحد ذلك لا يجوز، دل أن المانع ما ذكرنا من استحالة قيام ومع البقاء به فيشاركه جميع ما يستحيل ذلك عليه، ولأن الحكميات هي التي تعتبر الحاجة وعدم الحاجة لا الحقائق بل يجب النظر فيها إلى ما يوجب التغير أو إلى الوجود والعدم، فإذا هذا التعليل منه جهل مفرط.
And it is said to al-Jabba'i and his son: If the persisting thing is persistent by itself, then what prevented permitting the persistence of sounds, pains, movements, thoughts, volitions, dislikes, and annihilation? Likewise it is said to Abu Hashim: What prevented permitting the persistence of speech? And he refines this and says: Whatever thing is not valid for an attribute to occur in it at its first (inception) or by it, it is not valid for that attribute after existence, such as these accidents which you have agreed with us on the impossibility of stating their persistence. And if we said that accidents persist, the attribute would change upon them because we have shown that they are not described by persistence in the state of occurrence. And there is no meaning to the statement of the Bahshamiyya that speech is not annihilated only because of the need to interrupt it and follow it with its kind in the form of composition and arrangement, because what follows it, if it is not an opposite for it existing in its locus, does not necessitate preventing its persistence. And because if the preventing cause for its persistence were this, then the persistence of a single letter would be permissible, but that is not permissible. This indicates that the preventing cause is what we mentioned: the impossibility of subsistence with persistence in it, and all that for which that is impossible shares in this. And because rulings are those that consider need and lack of need, not realities, but rather one must look in them to what necessitates change or to existence and non-existence. So this rationalization of his is extreme ignorance.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a series of questions and refutations directed at the Mu'tazilites (al-Jabba'i, his son, Abu Hashim, and the Bahshamiyya). First, the author asks: if a persisting thing persists by itself, why not permit the persistence of sounds, pains, movements, thoughts, etc., which you deny? Similarly, why not permit persistence of speech? He then states a principle: an attribute that cannot characterize something at its inception (first moment) cannot characterize it later. Since these accidents are not described as persistent at their occurrence, they cannot become persistent later. He then refutes the Bahshamiyya's claim that speech does not persist because it needs to be interrupted and followed by similar speech. If that were the reason, a single letter could persist, which they deny. The real reason is that persistence is incompatible with the nature of such accidents. This shows the Bahshamiyya's reasoning is ignorance.

Key Terms
جبائي
أبو هاشم
بهشمية
بقاء
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That speech does not persist because it needs to be interrupted and followed by similar speech for composition.
Correction: If that were true, a single letter, which needs no interruption, could persist, but the Bahshamiyya deny that. The real reason is the inherent impossibility of persistence for such accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the Bahshamiyya claim about speech?
A) Speech persists like all accidents.
B) Speech cannot persist because it must be interrupted and followed by similar speech.
C) Speech is not an accident.
D) Speech is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Accidents like sounds and speech cannot persist because the attribute of persistence is absent at their inception; the Bahshamiyya's need-based argument collapses under counterexample.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience countless fleeting thoughts and emotions daily—each one an accident that arises and vanishes instantly. If you truly believed that nothing in your inner world is permanent, how would that change the way you respond to anger, desire, or despair? What would it mean to hold onto none of them as 'you'?
?
The Bahshamiyya blamed interruption for the non-persistence of speech, but the source says the cause is inherent. Think of a sin you repeatedly commit. Do you blame it on external circumstances (interruptions) or on something broken within you? What would it cost you to admit that the problem is not your situation but your heart?
?
The principle here is that what is not persistent at inception cannot become persistent later. Consider your own worship: do you treat your salah or dhikr as a fleeting accident—something that ends when you finish—or as something that should persist through your day? If it vanishes the moment you finish, what does that say about its reality in your heart? How will you change so that your worship leaves a lasting trace?

Why Accidents Cannot Remain: A Maturidi Critique
The author questions al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim about why accidents cannot remain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that al-Jubba'i's position on the impossibility of accidents remaining is inconsistent with his own claims.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot remain, not because of their nature, but because their description changes.

The author here engages in a dialectical critique of al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim, challenging their denial of the possibility of accidents remaining. He points out an inconsistency: if a substance remains by itself, why can't accidents also remain? He then refines a general principle about the validity of descriptions after existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial analogy
Read the part about God's command 'Perish' and its implication for multiple times. This sets up the logic of permissibility.
Note that the argument from annihilation is used to show that if something is possible once, it is possible many times.
2
Examine the challenge to al-Jubba'i
Identify the question: if a substance remains by itself, why cannot accidents like sounds, pains, etc., remain?
This challenges the claim that accidents are inherently non-remaining.
3
Examine the challenge to Abu Hashim
Find the specific question to Abu Hashim about speech. Why cannot speech remain?
This extends the critique to another prominent Mu'tazili.
4
Grasp the refined principle
Understand the general rule: if something cannot have a certain description at its beginning, it cannot have that description after existence.
This principle is used to argue that accidents cannot be described as remaining because they are not so at the moment of occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول الله تعالى له: "افن" لما جاز زمانًا واحدًا ولم يتنافيا جاز في أزمنة كثيرة، وليس الفناء في الثاني والثالث بأولى منه في الأول. ويقال للجبائي وابنه إذا كان الباقي باقيًا لذاته، فما الذي منع من القول بجواز بقاء الصوات والآلام والحركات والفكر والإرادات والكراهات والفناء؟ وكذا يقال لأبي هاشم: ما الذي منع من القول بجواز بقاء الكلام؟ ويحرر هذا، ويقول: ما لا يصح حدوث شيء فيه أوله أو به لم يصح بغير الوصف عليه بعد الوجود، كهذه الأعراض التي وافقتمونا على استحالة القول ببقائها، ولو قلنا ببقاء الأعراض لتغير الوصف عليها لما بينا أنها في حالة الحدوث ليس بموصوفة بالبقاء.
The saying of Allah the Exalted to him: 'Perish' when it was permissible for a single time and they did not contradict each other, it is permissible in many times, and the annihilation in the second and third is not more likely than in the first. And it is said to al-Jubba'i and his son: If the remaining (al-bāqī) is remaining by itself, then what prevented the statement of the permissibility of the remaining of sounds, pains, movements, thoughts, wills, aversions, and annihilation? And likewise it is said to Abu Hashim: What prevented the statement of the permissibility of the remaining of speech? And this is refined, and he says: That in which the occurrence of something in its beginning or through it is not valid, it is not valid without the description upon it after existence, like these accidents that you have agreed with us on the impossibility of the statement of their remaining. And if we said with the remaining of accidents, the description upon them would change, because we have shown that they are not described with perpetuity in the state of occurrence.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage begins with a reference to God's command 'Perish' to something, arguing that if annihilation is permissible for a single time without contradiction, then it is permissible for many times, and there is no priority of annihilation in the second or third over the first. Then the author addresses al-Jubba'i and his son (Abu Hashim): since they claim that the remaining substance (al-bāqī) remains by itself (i.e., its permanence is intrinsic), then why do they deny the possibility of accidents remaining? The author lists examples of accidents: sounds, pains, movements, thoughts, wills, aversions, and even annihilation (al-fanā'). Similarly, they ask Abu Hashim why speech cannot remain. The author then formulates a general principle: if something cannot have a beginning (ḥudūth) in a certain manner (either in its first moment or through a certain cause), then after existence it cannot have a different description. The agreed-upon accidents are such that both sides agree they cannot remain; to say they remain would change their description, since in their state of occurrence they are not characterized by perpetuity (al-baqā').

Key Terms
الباقي (al-bāqī)
بقاء (al-baqā')
فناء (al-fanā')
أعراض (a'rāḍ)
الجبائي (al-Jubba'i)
أبو هاشم (Abu Hāshim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the impossibility of accidents remaining is due to their contingency or intrinsic nature.
Correction: The author argues that the impossibility is due to a change in description: accidents are not characterized by perpetuity at the moment of occurrence, so they cannot acquire it later.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the question directed at al-Jubba'i and his son?
A) Why can't substances remain by themselves?
B) Why can't accidents remain if substances remain by themselves?
C) Why is annihilation impossible?
D) What prevents speech from remaining?

Key Takeaway
The author's critique shows that if substances remain by themselves, there is no reason to deny accidents remaining, except that it would change their description from occurrence to perpetuity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that an accident cannot acquire a new description after its occurrence. Consider a persistent negative feeling or thought you have. Are you assuming it will eventually change on its own? What does this principle say about that assumption, and what action does it call you to take?
?
In the passage, even the Mu'tazila who denied accident remaining were inconsistent. Where in your own life do you claim something is temporary (like a bad habit) but act as if it will last forever? What would it mean to truly treat it as an accident that cannot remain?
?
If you truly believed that every created pain, desire, or thought cannot permanently remain, how would that change your reaction to them right now? Would you cling less or fight more? Write down one specific way you will apply this insight today.

Refutation of the Bahshamiyya on the Non-Remaining of Speech
The author refutes the Bahshamiyya's argument that speech does not remain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Bahshamiyya's argument that speech does not remain is invalid, and the correct reasoning about the impossibility of accidents subsisting with perpetuity.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot remain; succession is not the cause.

The Bahshamiyya claimed that speech does not remain because it must be cut off and followed by another letter in a specific order. The author refutes this by showing that the real prevention is the impossibility of an accident persisting over time, not the need for succession. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then its literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through before analyzing.
The passage contains multiple refutations in a single paragraph.
2
Identify the Bahshamiyya's claim and the counterarguments
Find the Bahshamiyya's stated reason for speech not remaining, then list the three counterarguments the author provides: (1) following is not an opposite, (2) the single-letter reductio, (3) the confusion of hukmiyyat and haqa'iq.
3
Understand the distinction between hukmiyyat and haqa'iq
Reflect on why juridical considerations (hukmiyyat) are not applicable to real essences (haqa'iq). The author emphasizes that in realities, one must consider what necessitates change or existence and non-existence.
This distinction is crucial for proper theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا معنى لقول البهشمية إن الكلام إنما لا ينفي للحاجة إلى قطعه واتباعه بجنسه على صورة النظم والتأليف، لأن ما يتبعه إذا لم يكن ضدا له حاصلا في محله لا يوجب المنع من بقائه؛ ولأن العلة المانعة عن بقائه لو كان هذا لكان يجوز بقاء الحرف الواحد ذلك لا يجوز، دل أن المانع ما ذكرنا من استحالة قيام ومع البقاء به فيشاركه جميع ما يستحيل ذلك عليه، ولأن الحكميات هي التي تعتبر الحاجة وعدم الحاجة لا الحقائق بل يجب النظر فيها إلى ما يوجب التغير أو إلى الوجود والعدم، فإذا هذا التعليل منه جهل مفرط.
And there is no meaning to the saying of the Bahshamiyya that speech does not remain because of the need to cut it and follow it with its genus in the form of arrangement and composition, because that which follows it, if it is not an opposite (ḍidd) occurring in its locus, does not necessitate the prevention of its remaining; and because if the prevention cause (al-ʿilla al-māniʿa) for its remaining were this, then the remaining of a single letter would be permissible, but that is not permissible, indicating that the prevention is what we mentioned of the impossibility of [the accident] subsisting with perpetuity (al-baqāʾ), so all that for which that is impossible shares it. And because the juridical considerations (al-ḥukmiyyāt) are those that consider need and lack of need, not the realities (al-ḥaqāʾiq); rather, one must consider in them what necessitates change or existence and non-existence. So this reasoning from him is extreme ignorance.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage refutes the Bahshamiyya sect's argument that speech (al-kalām) does not remain because it needs to be cut off and followed by another of its kind in a specific order (nazm and ta'līf). The author argues that if something follows it and is not an opposite (ḍidd) occurring in the same locus, that does not prevent its remaining. Moreover, if the reason for non-remaining were the need for succession, then a single letter (ḥarf wāḥid) could remain — but it does not, proving that the true prevention is the impossibility of an accident subsisting with perpetuity (al-baqā'). The author further criticizes the Bahshamiyya for confusing juridical considerations (al-ḥukmiyyāt) with real essences (al-ḥaqā'iq); in the latter, one must consider what necessitates change or existence and non-existence. The author concludes that this reasoning is extreme ignorance.

كلام
ضد
علة مانعة
حكميات
حقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the need for succession (cutting and following) is the reason speech does not remain.
Correction: The real preventing cause is the impossibility of an accident subsisting with perpetuity, not the need for succession.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to refute the Bahshamiyya's argument about speech?
A) The impossibility of a single letter remaining.
B) The need for speech to be followed.
C) A Qur'anic verse.
D) The consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Bahshamiyya's reasoning is invalid because the real prevention of speech's remaining is the impossibility of an accident subsisting eternally, not the need for succession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a position with reasoning that turned out to be based on a category mistake—applying the wrong kind of logic to a profound truth? What was that position, and how did you come to see your error? Commit to one specific step you will take today to examine your beliefs more rigorously.
?
The author calls the Bahshamiyya's reasoning 'extreme ignorance' because they confused need-based juridical considerations with the nature of realities. Is there an area in your life where you are treating a deep, unchanging truth as if it were a matter of convenience or personal preference? What would it look like to submit to that truth instead of adapting it to your needs?
?
If the impossibility of accidents remaining is a universal principle, what does that say about your attachment to temporary things—your achievements, your reputation, your comforts? How can you prepare your heart today for what truly remains?

The Opponents' Question on Perpetuity
Opponents' dilemma on perpetuity with origin in motion argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by the opponents about the timing of perpetuity and its origin in the atheists' argument against motion.

Core Idea
Perpetuity's timing dilemma mirrors the atheists' attack on motion.

The passage opens with a sharp objection: if a substance remains by a perpetuity, when does that perpetuity occur? The author reveals that this is not a new problem—it is borrowed from the atheists (al-mulḥidūn) who used a similar argument to deny motion. By examining this parallel, we see the foundation of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' dilemma
Read the Arabic text and translation to grasp the structure of the question: perpetuity must occur either in the first moment or the second, each leading to a contradiction.
Pay attention to the two possibilities.
2
Identify the forced conclusions
If perpetuity occurs in the first moment, the substance would be remaining from the start; if in the second, it does not remain from the first—an absurdity.
The opponents aim to show that 'remaining by a perpetuity' is impossible.
3
Recognize the origin in the motion argument
The author notes this question is taken from the atheists' question about motion, which tries to negate accidents. Read that analogy carefully.
The same two-option structure applies to motion.
4
Ask why this parallel matters
Consider: By tracing the question to the atheists, the author highlights the threat it poses to the reality of all accidents, not just perpetuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر أسئلتهم التي يعولون عليها في إبطال قولنا، فمن ذلك قولهم: إن الباقي لو كان باقيًا ببقاء لم يخل البقاء من أن يكون حادثا في حال حدوثه أو في الثاني، فإن حدث في حال حدوث الجوهر وجب أن يكون باقيا فيه لوجود البقاء له، وإن كان حادثا في الثاني لم يبق به من الأول. والجواب عنه: أن هذا سؤال مأخوذ من سؤال الملحدين في نفي الأعراض، وهو قولهم: لو كان الجسم متحركًا بحركة لم تخل حركتها من أن تكون حادثة في المكان الأول أو تكون حادثة وهو في المكان الثاني، فإن حدثت الحركة والجسم في المكان الأول لكانت الحركة موجودة والجسم ليس بمتحرك ولم يجب خروجه عن مكانه لوجودها، وإن حدثت وهي في المكان الثاني استغن بكونه فيه عن حركة ينتقل بها إليه. ثم من أصحابنا من أجاب عن السؤالين جميعًا بجواب وحد وسوى بين الحركة والبقاء وقال: إن الحركة تحدث والجسم في المكان الثاني والبقاء يحدث في الوقت الثاني، وإنما كان كذلك لأن المتحرك إنما يكون متحركا بخروجه عن الأول وكونه في الثاني، واحتاج إلى حدوث الكون فيه لأن خروجه عن الأول نفى لكونه فيه ولا يحتاج إلى الكون في مكان ليس بكائن فيه. وهذا المذهب في الحركة مذهب أبي الهذيل وجماعة من المعتزلة، وإليه ذهب الأشعري. والباقي يكون باقيًا في الوقت الثاني واحتاج إلى البقاء فيه وليس ببقاء عن الأول إلى الثاني، وإنما يكون محدثًا في الأول غير باق ثم يكون باقيًا في الثاني بحدوث البقاء فيه. ومنهم من فرق بين الحركة والبقاء، وأجاب عن سؤال الحركة أن ليس باسم لعرض واحد بل هو اسم لعرضين وهما كونان أحدهما في المكان الأول، والآخر في المكان الثاني، فما لم يوجد الثاني لا يكون متحركًا فلا تكون الحركة موجودة في المكان الأول ولا في الثاني بل يتم في الثاني، وإليه ذهب ابن الراوندي في آخر عمره، وهو مذهب أبي العباس القلانسي وجماعة من المتكلمين: يتبين ذلك بعد هذا إن شاء الله تعالى.
Then we mention their questions that they rely on in invalidating our statement. Among them is their saying: If the remaining (al-bāqī) is remaining by a perpetuity (bi-baqā'), the perpetuity is not free from being either occurring (ḥādith) in the moment of its occurrence or in the second. If it occurs in the moment of the occurrence of the substance, then it must be remaining in it due to the existence of perpetuity for it. And if it occurs in the second, then it does not remain by it from the first. The answer to it: This question is taken from the question of the atheists (al-mulḥidīn) in negating accidents. It is their saying: If a body were moving by a motion, the motion is not free from being occurring in the first place or occurring while it is in the second place. If the motion occurs while the body is in the first place, then the motion would exist and the body would not be moving, and it would not be necessary for it to leave its place due to its existence. And if it occurs while it is in the second place, then it would be independent by its being in it from a motion by which it transfers to it. Then among our companions are those who answered both questions with one answer and equated between motion and perpetuity, saying: Motion occurs while the body is in the second place, and perpetuity occurs in the second time. It is like that because the mover is only moving by its leaving the first and being in the second, and it needs the occurrence of being (al-kawn) in it because its leaving the first negates its being in it, and it does not need being in a place in which it is not. This doctrine of motion is the doctrine of Abu al-Hudhayl and a group of the Mu'tazila, and al-Ash'ari adopted it. And the remaining (al-bāqī) is remaining in the second time and needs perpetuity in it, and it is not a perpetuity from the first to the second; rather, it is originated (muḥdath) in the first, not remaining, then it becomes remaining in the second by the occurrence of perpetuity in it. And among them are those who differentiated between motion and perpetuity, and answered the question of motion that it is not a name for a single accident but a name for two accidents, which are two 'beings' (kawnān), one in the first place and the other in the second place. So as long as the second has not come into existence, it is not moving, so the motion does not exist in the first place nor in the second; rather, it is completed in the second. This was adopted by Ibn al-Rawandi in the latter part of his life, and it is the doctrine of Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi and a group of theologians. This will become clear after this, God willing.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a key objection from the opponents (likely the Mu'tazila) against the Maturidi claim that an existing thing remains by a perpetuity (baqā') that is an accident. They ask: if a substance remains by a perpetuity, is that perpetuity happening at the first moment (when the substance is originated) or in the second moment? If it is in the first moment, then the substance must be remaining from the first moment. If it is in the second moment, then it does not remain by that perpetuity from the first moment. The author states that this question is based on a similar question used by atheists (al-mulḥidūn) to negate accidents. The atheists asked about motion: if a body moves by a motion, does the motion occur in the first place or the second? If in the first, the body would not actually be moving; if in the second, it wouldn't need motion to get there. The author then presents two answers from the Ahl al-Sunna: (1) Some scholars, including Abu al-Hudhayl (a Mu'tazili) and al-Ash'ari, answered both questions by equating motion and perpetuity. They say motion occurs while the body is already in the second place; similarly, perpetuity occurs in the second time. The being (kawn) in the second place is needed because leaving the first negates being there, but being in the second is not needed because without it there is no being. On this view, the substance is originated (muḥdath) in the first moment, not remaining, then it becomes remaining in the second by the occurrence of perpetuity. (2) Other scholars, such as Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, differentiated between motion and perpetuity. They argue that 'motion' is not a single accident but the name for two accidents: a being (kawn) in the first place and a being in the second place. Until the second being occurs, the body is not moving. Thus motion is not present in the first or second but is completed in the second. The author notes this will be clarified later.

Key Terms
باقي (bāqī)
بقاء (baqā')
حركة (ḥaraka)
كون (kawn)
ملحدين (mulḥidīn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think perpetuity necessarily occurs at the same time as the substance's existence.
Correction: The opponents force a choice: either it occurs in the first moment (making the substance remaining from the start) or in the second (leaving the first moment without perpetuity). Both options are problematic for the Ahl al-Sunna position.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, from where is the question about perpetuity derived?
A) From the Mu'tazila directly.
B) From the questions of the atheists (al-mulḥidīn) about motion.
C) From the philosophers.
D) From the Ash'arites.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' dilemma on perpetuity is a direct application of the atheists' argument against motion, aiming to show that attributing existence to accidents leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue about theological matters, do you ever unknowingly use the same logical tactics as those who deny God? Reflect on a recent discussion—was your argument grounded in revelation or borrowed from a skeptic's playbook? Commit to checking the source of your reasoning.
?
The opponent's dilemma shows that even sophisticated logic can lead to absurdity if built on false premises. Can you identify an area in your own faith where you have relied on human reasoning at the expense of divine text? What step will you take today to realign your understanding with revelation?
?
The atheists' argument against motion is used by Muslims against each other. This should humble you. Where in your heart have you used a tool of the skeptic against a fellow believer? Repent and resolve to rely on the Quran and Sunnah as your primary authority, not borrowed logic.
