Refuting Abu Hashim on Eternal Emptiness
The author refutes Abu Hashim's reconciliation by rational intuition and causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hashim's attempt to allow a capable agent to remain eternally without action is rationally impossible and contradicts the nature of cause and effect.

Core Idea
A capable agent cannot remain empty of voluntary action eternally—intuition and causality prove it.

Abu Hashim, a Mu'tazili theologian, tried to escape a dilemma by allowing that a capable agent could remain forever without performing any act or its opposite. The author refutes this by appealing to rational intuition and the principle that a cause cannot be empty of its effect. This passage shows how kalam arguments ground themselves in basic rational principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The background: the Mu'tazila faced an objection leading to a dilemma. Abu Hashim sought to resolve it by equalizing two options and permitting eternal non-action.
2
Read Abu Hashim's position
Read the first part of the Arabic passage carefully: 'Abu Hashim equalized between the two matters and permitted the emptiness of the capable...' Understand what he claimed.
Notice the conditions: faculties present, no impediments, yet no action or its opposite.
3
Follow the refutation
The author gives two arguments: first, by rational intuition (every rational person knows emptiness from voluntary motion is impossible); second, by causality (capacity runs with cause from effect, so a cause cannot be empty of its effect).
The causality argument is also a starting point for a proof of the author's own school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما رأى أبو هاشم توجه هذا الإلزام عليهم سوى بين الأمرين، وأجاز خلو القادر - مع توفر آلاته وانعدام الموانع أبد الدهر عن الفعل والترك لا يفعل فعلاً ولا يباشر مأمورًا ولا ضده الذي هو تركه ولا يرتكب منهيًا ولا ضده الذي هو تركه.
وهذا مما يعرفه كل عاقل ببديهة عقله أن خلو القادر عن الحركة والسكون الاختياريين محال ممتنع مع هذا هو قول باطل هادم لقواعد مذهبه، وذلك لأن الاستطاعة تجري مع العلة من المعلول، وهو ابتداء دليل لنا في وخلو العلة عن المعلول ممتنع، وفيما قاله أبو هاشم خلوها عنه أبد
When Abu Hashim saw the force of this objection against them, he equalized between the two matters, and permitted the emptiness of the capable (al-qadir) - with the presence of his faculties and the absence of impediments forever from action and abandonment - that he does not do an act nor perform a commanded act nor its opposite which is its abandonment, nor does he commit a forbidden act nor its opposite which is its abandonment. And this is something that every rational person knows by the intuition of his intellect: that the emptiness of the capable from voluntary motion and rest is impossible, and with this is a false statement that demolishes the foundations of his school. That is because capacity (al-istita'a) runs with the cause from the effect, and it is a beginning of a proof for us that the emptiness of the cause from the effect is impossible, and in what Abu Hashim said, its emptiness from it forever.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage refutes Abu Hashim's attempt to reconcile the earlier objection. The objection forced the Mu'tazila into a dilemma; Abu Hashim tried to escape by allowing that the capable agent could remain forever without performing any act or its opposite, despite having all means and no impediments. The author declares this view impossible by rational intuition (badiha al-'aql): every rational person knows that the capable agent cannot be empty of voluntary motion or rest. Furthermore, the author connects this to his own proof: capacity (istita'a) runs along with the cause from the effect, meaning that a cause cannot be empty of its effect. Abu Hashim's view implies emptiness forever, which is impossible. This refutation demolishes Abu Hashim's school's foundations.

Key Terms
أبو هاشم (Abu Hashim)
قادر (qadir)
خلو (khuluww)
الحركة والسكون الاختياريين (voluntary motion and rest)
استطاعة (istita'a)
علة (illa)
معلول (ma'lul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A capable agent can eternally refrain from any action despite having full ability and no obstacles.
Correction: The author argues that rational intuition and the principle of cause and effect both show this is impossible; a capable agent with full ability cannot remain empty of voluntary motion or rest.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does every rational person know that Abu Hashim's view is false?
A) Through revelation
B) By intuition of the intellect
C) By consensus of scholars
D) By sensory experience

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's reconciliation is impossible because rational intuition and the principle of causality both require that a capable agent with full ability cannot remain eternally without action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to be a capable agent (qadir) with free will, but have you ever excused your own inaction by reasoning that 'I just didn't choose to' for so long it became a habit? What if that very reasoning is the same emptiness Abu Hashim defended—a deception that you can eternally postpone what Allah demands? Today, identify one commanded act you have avoided 'just because' and commit to performing it before the day ends, realizing that your capacity itself is a debt you owe to your Creator.
?
If every rational person knows by intuition that a capable agent cannot remain empty of action, why do you still find yourself in a state of spiritual inertia—neither actively obeying nor openly disobeying, but simply coasting? Could it be that you have internalized Abu Hashim's false equalization, believing you are excused from both sin and worship? What would it mean for your life today to reject that false neutrality and choose an active posture of obedience?
?
The author argues that capacity (istita'a) runs with the cause from the effect—you cannot have ability without using it. Think of one talent, one opportunity, or one breath Allah has given you today. Have you used it for His sake, or have you let it lie empty? What is the cost to your soul of treating His gifts as optional? Name one concrete way you will 'cause' an effect for His pleasure before you sleep tonight.

Reward and Punishment Require Actions, Not Omissions
Nasafi refutes a claim about reward/punishment based on absence of deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reward and punishment in Islamic theology are contingent upon actual deeds, not mere absence of sin or obedience.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment follow actions, not omissions.

Nasafi confronts a controversial claim that God punishes or rewards based on what a person does not do—absence of sin or obedience. He exposes the contradiction and redefines the terms 'thawab' and 'iqab' to ground them in positive acts. This passage is a clear lesson in theological precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the passage and pinpoint the assertion Nasafi is refuting: that God punishes without evil deeds and rewards without good deeds.
Notice how Nasafi immediately calls this a contradiction.
2
Understand the contradiction
Nasafi says the opponent himself would declare others disbelievers for the same view. Ask: Why is this contradictory?
3
Define reward and punishment correctly
Focus on Nasafi's definitions: thawab follows a good deed, iqab follows an evil deed. These are not based on omissions.
The etymological point (thawab from 'return of benefit') reinforces the link to action.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this principle—actions determine requital—upholds justice and coherence in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعم أن الله تعالى يخلد الإنسان في النار لا على فعل قبيح ارتكبه ولا جناية سبقت منه، ويعطي الجنان لا على فعل اكتسبه ولا طاعة فعلت منه، وهذا هو عين ما أباه وأكفر به غيره وهو غاية التناقض. وأعجب من ذلك كله أن يسمى ما أعطى لانعدام المعاصي منه ثوابًا وإن كان الثواب جزاء ما سبق منه من الفعل الحسن سمى به لعود منفعة عمله إليه، ويسمى ما أولم به لانعدام المأمور به عقابًا، وإن كان العقاب ما يتعقب الفعل القبيح من الجزاء المؤلم سمى عقابًا لتعقبه ذلك الفعل، وهذا منه جهل بحقيقة الثواب والعقاب.
Then he claimed that God Most High eternally punishes a person in the Fire not for an evil deed he committed nor for any crime that preceded from him, and He grants Gardens not for an act he performed nor for any obedience done by him. This is precisely what he himself rejected and declared others disbelievers for, and it is the ultimate contradiction. And more astonishing than all that is that he calls what is given for the absence of sins from him 'reward' (thawab), even though reward is the requital for a good deed that preceded from him, so named because the benefit of his action returns to him. And he calls what is inflicted for the absence of what was commanded 'punishment' ('iqab), even though punishment is what follows an evil deed from painful requital, so named because it follows that deed. This from him is ignorance of the reality of reward and punishment.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent (likely Abu Hashim al-Jubbai) who claims that God rewards people for avoiding sins without performing good deeds, and punishes them for failing to perform obligatory acts without committing evil deeds. Nasafi points out the contradiction: the opponent himself would declare others disbelievers for holding such a view. He then defines reward (thawab) as requital for a good deed already performed, and punishment ('iqab) as requital that follows an evil deed. Calling mere absence of sin 'reward' or absence of obedience 'punishment' is ignorance of these terms' true meanings. The passage proceeds by stating the opponent's claim, identifying it as contradictory, and then correcting the definitions of thawab and 'iqab.

Key Terms
ثواب
عقاب
جزاء
فعل حسن
فعل قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that avoiding sin is itself a virtuous act worthy of reward, or that failing to perform an obligatory act is itself worthy of punishment, regardless of positive actions.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that reward strictly follows a good deed performed, and punishment follows an evil deed committed. Omissions are not the basis for requital; actions are.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the correct definition of 'reward' (thawab)?
A) It is given for the absence of sins.
B) It is the requital for a good deed already performed by the person.
C) It is given arbitrarily by God's will.
D) It is the same as punishment.

Key Takeaway
Reward and punishment in Islamic theology are always tied to actual actions—good deeds earn reward, evil deeds earn punishment—not to mere absences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life, do you often feel entitled to reward simply because you avoid major sins? How does Nasafi’s insistence that reward requires actual good deeds challenge your sense of security? Will you commit to performing at least one extra act of obedience today as a concrete step?
?
Think of an area where you have been neglecting a commanded action—perhaps prayer, charity, or seeking knowledge. Have you ever justified that by saying 'at least I'm not sinning'? How does this passage expose that reasoning as ignorance? What specific action will you take this week to correct that omission?
?
The opponent in this passage misused the terms 'reward' and 'punishment'. In what ways might you be using religious language carelessly, applying labels like 'blessing' or 'test' to situations without connecting them to actual deeds? What would it take for your speech to align with the precise meanings taught here?

The Maturidi Consensus: Command as Prohibition
Nasafi states the Maturidi consensus and its basis in the unity of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school holds that command over a thing is identical to prohibition of its opposite, and how this is rooted in the nature of divine speech.

Core Idea
Command and its opposite are two sides of the same divine speech.

Nasafi begins by presenting the agreed position of the Maturidi scholars: every commanded act has a specific opposite whose performance is prohibited. This may seem like a logical point, but it is fundamentally linked to their doctrine of divine speech as a single, undivided attribute. This lesson unpacks that view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi consensus
Read the first part of the passage where Nasafi states the consensus that abandonment of a commanded act (i.e., doing its opposite) is prohibited, and vice versa.
Notice the phrase 'من الجانين جميعًا'.
2
Understand the key phrase
Identify the statement: 'command over a thing is prohibition of its opposite.' Ask yourself why this follows from the unity of divine speech.
3
Connect to divine speech
Reflect on the explanation: because God's speech is one, it both commands and prohibits simultaneously.
This is the core of the Maturidi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أصحابنا مع أوائلهم اتفقوا أن كل مأمور به كان تركه وهو فعل مضادة منهيًا عنه، وكل منهي عنه تركه وهو فعل مضادة مأمورا به، إذا كان لكل واحد منهما ترك مخصوص وضد متعين، وكذا عندنا في كل ما له أضداد من الجانين جميعًا، وعندهم فيما له أضداد تقسيم يطول ذكره يعرف ذلك في أصول الفقه، أن عندنا كان الأمر بالشيء نهيًا عن ضده، والنهي عن الشيء أمرًا بضده، إذ كلام الله تعالى عندنا واحد وهو بنفسه أمر بما أمر نهي عما نهى، فكان ما هو الأمر بالشيء نهيًا عن ضده. وعند المعتزلة كلام الله تعالى هذه العبارات المتركبة من الحروف على هذه الصيغات وللأمر صيغة مخصوصة وكذا للنهي صيغة مخصوصة لا يتصور كون النهي أمرًا ولا كون الأمر نهيًا. ولا شك أن ضد المأمور منهي وضد المنهي مأمور. واختلفت عباراتهم في ذلك فزعم بعضهم: أن الأمر بالشيء يدل على النهي عن ضده، والنهي عن الشيء يدل على الأمر بضده. وقال بعضهم: الأمر بالشيء يقتضي نهيًا عن ضده وكذا على القلب. ومنهم من يطلق ما يتفق عليه من اللفظ ولا يفرق بين لفظة الدلالة ولفظة الاقتضاء على طريق التساهل. فلما تحير هذا الرجل فى هذه المسألة وتجاهل، وزعم أن ترك الزنا ليس بمأمور به وترك الصلاة ليس بمنهي عنه، زعم أن الأمر بالشيء ليس بنهي عن ضده، ولا يدل عليه ولا يقتضيه، ولا حكم له في الضد، ويبقى الضد على ما كان قبل ورود الأمر، وكذا في جانب النهي. وزعم أن ثواب من انعدم منه الزنا لا بمقابلة فعل ضده، وعقاب من انعدمت منه الصلاة لا بمقابلة فعل ضده. ثم إن بعض المتكلمين ممن تكلم في أصول الفقه من أهل ديارنا ذكر أني أقول: إن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده لا نهيه ولا أقول إنه نهى عن ضده ولا أقول إنه يدل على نهيه ولا أنه يقتضي نهيًا. ولست أدري ماذا كان رأيه في الكلام؟ أنه هو المعنى القائم بالذات المنافي للسكوت والآفة وهو بعينه أمر بما أمر به نهي عما نهى عنه كما هو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة. أو هو هذه الحروف المنظومة والأصوات المقطوعة، وما كان أمرًا لا يكون نهيًا، وما كان نهيًا لا يكون أمرًا كما هو مذهب المعتزلة؟ وكذا ما كان رأيه أن توجه الوعيد
Then indeed our companions, along with their early authorities, agreed that every commanded thing — its abandonment, which is the performance of its opposite, is prohibited; and every prohibited thing — its abandonment, which is the performance of its opposite, is commanded, since each of them has a specific abandonment and a determinate opposite. Likewise according to us in everything that has opposites from both sides; and according to them regarding what has opposites there is a classification whose mention is lengthy, known in usul al-fiqh. That according to us, command over a thing is prohibition of its opposite, and prohibition over a thing is command of its opposite, since according to us the speech of God Most High is one, and He Himself commands what He commands and prohibits what He prohibits, so that which is command over a thing is prohibition of its opposite. According to the Mu'tazila, the speech of God Most High is these expressions composed of letters in these forms, and the command has a specific form, and likewise the prohibition has a specific form; it is inconceivable that the prohibition be a command or the command be a prohibition. And there is no doubt that the opposite of the commanded is prohibited and the opposite of the prohibited is commanded. And their expressions differed regarding that: some claimed that the command over a thing indicates (yadullu) the prohibition of its opposite, and the prohibition over a thing indicates the command of its opposite. Others said: the command over a thing necessitates (yaqtadi) a prohibition of its opposite, and vice versa. And among them are those who use whatever wording is agreed upon, and do not distinguish between the word 'indication' and the word 'necessitation' by way of laxity. When this man became confused in this matter and feigned ignorance, and claimed that abandoning zina is not commanded and abandoning prayer is not prohibited, he claimed that command over a thing is not a prohibition of its opposite, nor does it indicate it, nor necessitate it, nor does it have any ruling concerning the opposite; the opposite remains as it was before the command came, and likewise regarding prohibition. And he claimed that the reward of one from whom zina is absent is not in exchange for performing its opposite, and the punishment of one from whom prayer is absent is not in exchange for performing its opposite. Then one of the mutakallimun who spoke in usul al-fiqh from the people of our region mentioned that I say: the command over a thing necessitates dislike (karaha) of its opposite, not its prohibition; I do not say it is a prohibition of its opposite, nor do I say it indicates its prohibition, nor that it necessitates a prohibition. I do not know what his opinion was concerning speech: is it the meaning subsisting in the essence, contrary to silence and incapacity, which is itself command of what He commands and prohibition of what He prohibits, according to the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a? Or is it these arranged letters and articulated sounds, and what was a command is not a prohibition and what was a prohibition is not a command, according to the madhhab of the Mu'tazila? And likewise, what was his opinion concerning the direction of the threat...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage classifies and explains the relationship between command (amr) and its opposite (didd), and between prohibition (nahy) and its opposite, according to the Maturidi school (our companions) and the Mu'tazila. It begins by stating the consensus of the Maturidi scholars that every commanded act has a specific opposite; abandoning the commanded (i.e., performing its opposite) is prohibited, and abandoning the prohibited (i.e., performing its opposite) is commanded. This is because, for Ahl al-Sunna, God's speech is one single attribute (al-kalam al-nafsi) that is both commanding and prohibiting simultaneously. For the Mu'tazila, speech is composed of separate created utterances, so a command cannot be a prohibition. The passage then notes the different Mu'tazili formulations: some say command indicates (yadullu) prohibition of its opposite; others say it necessitates (yaqtadi) it; some use the terms loosely. The opponent (Abu Hashim) is criticized for denying any relationship between command and its opposite, and for claiming that reward for avoiding sin is not due to performing its opposite. Then a later mutakallim from the author's region is quoted as saying that command only implies dislike (karaha) of the opposite, not prohibition. The author questions this mutakallim's stance on the nature of divine speech: does he hold the Ahl al-Sunna view (single meaning) or the Mu'tazili view (composed letters)? The passage ends with an incomplete remark about 'the direction of the threat'.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
ضد
كلام الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That command and prohibition are separate utterances that logically imply each other.
Correction: For the Maturidis, it is not a logical implication but an identity due to the unity of divine speech.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view, why is command over a thing also prohibition of its opposite?
A) Because opposite actions are contradictory.
B) Because God's speech is one and commands and prohibits simultaneously.
C) Because the Mu'tazila agree on this point.
D) Because the command contains a separate prohibition utterance.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school holds that command over a thing is identical to prohibition of its opposite because God's speech is a single attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find it hard to obey a command of Allah, do you realize that the same divine speech that commands you also prohibits its opposite? How does this awareness change your struggle with obedience?
?
If you truly believed that God's prohibition of something is identical to His command of its opposite, would your attitude towards avoiding sins be different? What would you do today to act on that belief?
?
The Maturidi teach that command and prohibition are two sides of the same divine will. Does your obedience reflect that you see them as one? What area of your life shows a disconnect that you need to address?

Mu'tazili View and Its Variants
Nasafi explains the Mu'tazili view on the relationship between command and its opposite, and their differing formulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili position that command and prohibition are distinct utterances, and the different views among them on how command relates to its opposite.

Core Idea
For Mu'tazila, command and prohibition are separate utterances that cannot be identical.

Nasafi contrasts the Maturidi view with the Mu'tazili position. For the Mu'tazila, divine speech consists of created letters and sounds, so command and prohibition are distinct forms. This leads to a debate among them on whether command indicates (dalala) or necessitates (iqtida') prohibition of its opposite. This lesson explores those nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili position
Read the part where Nasafi states the Mu'tazili view that speech is composed letters and command has its own form, prohibition its own form.
2
Note the key difference
Contrast with the Maturidi view: for Mu'tazila, it is inconceivable that a command be a prohibition.
This is the central disagreement.
3
Review the three Mu'tazili variants
Identify the three groups: those who say command indicates (yadullu) prohibition of its opposite, those who say it necessitates (yaqtadi), and those who use the terms loosely.
Understand the difference between dalala and iqtida'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila agree that command implies prohibition of its opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, why can a command not be a prohibition?
A) Because God's speech is one.

The Mu'tazila view command and prohibition as distinct utterances due to their belief in created speech, leading to debates on whether command indicates or necessitates prohibition of its opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Mu'tazila debated whether a command 'indicates' or 'necessitates' the prohibition of its opposite, ask yourself: does your heart treat God's commands as mere suggestions that point to what to avoid, or do you feel the weight of necessity to obey? Identify one command you often treat lightly, and commit today to acting with the certainty that its opposite is forbidden.
?
The passage mentions a group who, 'by way of laxity', do not distinguish between 'indication' and 'necessitation'. Where in your own religious practice have you been imprecise with words or intentions? Choose one area—prayer, charity, or speech—and resolve to be exact, not lax.
?
Nasafi states without qualification: 'there is no doubt that the opposite of the commanded is prohibited and the opposite of the prohibited is commanded.' Does this truth govern your daily choices? When you know a command, do you immediately sense the prohibition of its neglect? Ask Allah to make your obedience stem from certainty, not forgetfulness.
